--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1735 (Number of characters: 118):
A DEFINITON OF TRAUMA-BASED CODEPENDENCY
More than a few of my clients were initially put off by the term codependent.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1736 (Number of characters: 110):
They found it
confusing or irrelevant because the descriptions they read or heard were presented derogatorily.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1737 (Number of characters: 77):
Moreover, some felt that the descriptions did not pertain to their condition.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1738 (Number of characters: 147):
If that is true for you,
the section below on codependent subtypes explains how the fawn response can manifest in a
variety of different behaviors.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1739 (Number of characters: 188):
I define trauma-induced codependency as a syndrome of self-abandonment and self-
abnegation. Codependency is a fear-based inability to express rights, needs and boundaries in
relationship.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1740 (Number of characters: 142):
It is a disorder of assertiveness, characterized by a dormant fight response and a
susceptibility to being exploited, abused and/or neglected.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1741 (Number of characters: 106):
In conversations, codependents seek safety and acceptance in relationship through listening
and eliciting.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1742 (Number of characters: 92):
They invite the other to talk rather than risk exposing their thoughts, views, and
feelings.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1743 (Number of characters: 214):
They ask questions to keep the attention off themselves, because their parents taught
them that talking was dangerous and that their words were indictments that would inevitably
prove them guilty of being unworthy.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1744 (Number of characters: 275):
The implicit code of the fawn type is that it is safer [1] to listen than to talk, [2] to agree
than to dissent, [3] to offer care than to ask for help, [4] to elicit the other than to express
yourself and [5] to leave choices to the other rather than to express preferences.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1745 (Number of characters: 119):
Sadly, the closest
that the unrecovered fawn type comes to getting his needs met is vicariously through helping
others.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1746 (Number of characters: 108):
Fawn types generally enhance their recovery by memorizing the list of Human Rights in
Toolbox 2, chapter 16.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1747 (Number of characters: 78):
I have this sardonic fantasy about two codependents going on their first date.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1748 (Number of characters: 98):
Somehow
they have agreed that they want to go to a movie, but how will they ever choose which one?

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1749 (Number of characters: 66):
“What do you want to see?” “Oh, I’m easy what do you want to see?”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1750 (Number of characters: 52):
“It really doesn’t matter to
me, I like everything.”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1751 (Number of characters: 30):
‘Me too why don’t you choose?”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1752 (Number of characters: 156):
“Oh I think it would be much better if
you pick.” “Oh I couldn’t, I never pick the right one.” “Me too, but I’m sure my pick would
definitely be the worst.”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1753 (Number of characters: 146):
And so it goes, ad infinitum, until it is too late to see any show, and

relieved at not having to put themselves out there, they call it a night.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1754 (Number of characters: 485):
CODEPENDENT SUBTYPES
I think a certain amount of the confusion about codependency can be clarified through
understanding the significant differences found in the three codependent subtypes that follow:
fawn-freeze, fawn-flight and fawn-fight. Fawn-Freeze: The Scapegoat
The fawn-freeze is typically the most codependently entrenched subtype. Not all scapegoats are
fawn-freeze, but since fawn and freeze types are both prone to extreme self-denial, many end up
in a scapegoat position.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1755 (Number of characters: 68):
This is also because these are the two most passive of the four F’s.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1756 (Number of characters: 113):
They have both typically
suffered the most punishment or rejection for asserting themselves in the toddler stage.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1757 (Number of characters: 135):
When the fawn-freeze is not able to escape the scapegoat role in childhood, she is then set
up to be similarly victimized in adulthood.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1758 (Number of characters: 96):
In worst case scenarios fawn-freezes are easily recognized by fight types who take them
captive.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1759 (Number of characters: 81):
They may then turn them into doormats and subject them to domestic violence [DV].

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1760 (Number of characters: 76):
Sometimes, the fawn-freeze does not even recognize that she is being abused.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1761 (Number of characters: 30):
Other times she
blames herself

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1762 (Number of characters: 29):
[as she had to in childhood].

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1763 (Number of characters: 180):
Moreover, as we know from studying the DV cycle, many narcissistic abusers know when
and how to shower romantic tidbits on their victims just when they are at the point of leaving.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1764 (Number of characters: 79):
These narcissists are often the charming bullies described in the last chapter.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1765 (Number of characters: 184):
Their infrequent
tidbits have more warmth in them than anything the codependent received at home, so she
quickly becomes re-hooked, and just as quickly the cycle of abuse begins again.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1766 (Number of characters: 189):
It is important to note that many charming bullies also offer copious tidbits briefly in the
courtship stage, but these peter out to near starvation rations once the entrapment is complete.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1767 (Number of characters: 95):
Many fawn-freeze types only make token efforts at recovery, if they do not avoid it
altogether.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1768 (Number of characters: 156):
Often fawn-freezes were forced to so thoroughly abandon their protective instincts
that they become trapped in what psychologists call learned helplessness.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1769 (Number of characters: 107):
Finally, there is growing evidence that a significant number of men also silently suffer
domestic violence.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1770 (Number of characters: 313):
A male client once told me that no matter how much his wife assaulted him,
he couldn’t stop himself from saying “I’m sorry” to her. This only made her madder, but not as
mad as when he flashbacked into saying: “I’m sorry for saying ‘I’m sorry’ “, even though his
wife would slap him in the face every time he did.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1771 (Number of characters: 133):
Not surprisingly, further investigation revealed a borderline mother who still slaps him in
the face when she is displeased with him.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1772 (Number of characters: 75):
As a child he was required to keep his hands down
whenever she slapped him.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1773 (Number of characters: 61):
He then had to apologize for making her “have to” punish him.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1774 (Number of characters: 164):
Unfortunately, he left therapy after only a few sessions because his wife looked in his
checkbook, and then hit him repeatedly for “wasting his time and her money.”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1775 (Number of characters: 105):
I have worked a great deal with these fawn-freeze types in my years of doing telephone
crisis counseling.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1776 (Number of characters: 98):
Hope for them lies in understanding how their childhood abuse set them up for
their current abuse.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1777 (Number of characters: 111):
This is often difficult, because scapegoated fawn-freezes were often punished

extra intensely for complaining.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1778 (Number of characters: 85):
Numerous times I have heard DV victims say: “But, I don’t want to act like a victim!”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1779 (Number of characters: 206):
Usually, I then try to help them see how much they truly were victims in childhood. However, if
I cannot get them to see this they usually are not able to rescue themselves from their current
victimization.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1780 (Number of characters: 224):
Fawn-Flight: Super Nurse
The fawn-flight type is most typically seen in the busyholic parent, nurse or administrative
assistant who works from dawn until bedtime providing for the needs of the household, hospital
or company.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1781 (Number of characters: 89):
He compulsively takes care of everyone else’s needs with hardly a gesture toward
his own.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1782 (Number of characters: 159):
The fawn-flight is sometimes a misguided Mother Teresa type, who escapes the pain of her
self-abandonment by seeing herself as the perfect, selfless caregiver.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1783 (Number of characters: 117):
She further distances
herself from her own pain by obsessive-compulsively rushing from one person in need to
another.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1784 (Number of characters: 60):
Some fawn-flight clients also become OCD-like clean-aholics.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1785 (Number of characters: 162):
One of my interns told me
that her fawn-flight client had a dozen color-coded tooth brushes for various micro-cleaning
tasks in her family’s bathroom and kitchen.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1786 (Number of characters: 56):
Some fawn-flights project their perfectionism on others.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1787 (Number of characters: 90):
They can appoint themselves as
honorary advisers, and overburden others with their advice.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1788 (Number of characters: 82):
However, it behooves fawn-flights to
learn that caring is not always about fixing.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1789 (Number of characters: 83):
This is especially true when the person we are trying
to help is in emotional pain.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1790 (Number of characters: 97):
Many times all that person needs is empathy, acceptance and an
opportunity to verbally ventilate.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1791 (Number of characters: 52):
Moreover some mood states also need time to resolve.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1792 (Number of characters: 69):
Loving
people when they are feeling bad is a powerful kind of caring.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1793 (Number of characters: 58):
The above also relates to allowing others to be imperfect.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1794 (Number of characters: 72):
We all have minor limitations and
foibles that may not be transformable.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1795 (Number of characters: 75):
Loved ones need to be spared from being pressured to fix
what is unfixable.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1796 (Number of characters: 80):
My way of approaching this is to always frame my advice as take-it or leave-
it.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1797 (Number of characters: 72):
To prove this is so, I refrain from then going on about it repetitively.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1798 (Number of characters: 97):
Additionally, I typically
check in first to see if the other person actually wants some feedback.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1799 (Number of characters: 137):
Fawn-Fight: Smother Mother
The recommendations in the last two paragraphs also apply to many of those survivors who are
fawn-fight types.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1800 (Number of characters: 62):
Some can be quite aggressive in their attempts to help others.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1801 (Number of characters: 122):
Typically they
equate helping with changing, and can alienate others by persistently pressuring them to take
their advice.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1802 (Number of characters: 50):
The fawn-fight type is the smother-love caretaker.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1803 (Number of characters: 117):
Her care-taking approach of being over-
focused on the other is sometimes a repetition of her childhood servant role.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1804 (Number of characters: 113):
Moreover, her
helpfulness is usually less self-serving than the fight-fawn that we discussed in the last chapter.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1805 (Number of characters: 129):
Nonetheless, the zealousness of her caretaking sometimes makes the recipient believe it when
she says “I just love you to death.”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1806 (Number of characters: 92):
In flashback, the fawn-fight can deteriorate into manipulative or even coercive care-taking.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1807 (Number of characters: 79):
He can smother love the other into conforming to his view of who she should be.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1808 (Number of characters: 157):
Fawn-fight types may periodically reach a critical mass of frustration that erupts when the
“patient” refuses his advice or balks at his unwanted caretaking.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1809 (Number of characters: 125):
Sometimes the fawn-fight feels
an entitlement to punish the other “for their own good”, especially in a primary relationship.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1810 (Number of characters: 82):
The fawn-fight is sometimes misdiagnosed as borderline personality disorder [BPD].

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1811 (Number of characters: 76):
This is
because the fawn-fight can be emotionally intense during flashbacks.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1812 (Number of characters: 167):
When triggered into a
panicky sense of abandonment, he feels desperate for love and can vacillate dramatically
between clawing for it or flatteringly groveling for it.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1813 (Number of characters: 110):
He does not however have the core
narcissism of the true borderline that we looked at in the previous chapter.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1814 (Number of characters: 88):
Another distinction between these two types is that fawn-fight type seeks real intimacy.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1815 (Number of characters: 210):
She
is the most relational hybrid and most susceptible to love addiction. She stands in
contradistinction to the fight-fawn who is more addicted to physical release, and hence more
susceptible to sex addiction.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1816 (Number of characters: 154):
MORE ON RECOVERING FROM A POLARIZED FAWN RESPONSE
Psychoeducation about their parents’ role in creating their fawn response has helped many of my
clients.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1817 (Number of characters: 169):
Many instantly grasped that their codependence comes from having been continuously
attacked and shamed as selfish for even the most basic level of healthy self-interest.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1818 (Number of characters: 165):
One fortyish client estimated that she had scorned herself as “selfish” countless times, until
one night she had the epiphany that she was by far not selfish enough.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1819 (Number of characters: 152):
This occurred while she
was reading the comments of respondents to a website for adult children of narcissistic mothers [
www.narcissisticmothers.org ].

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1820 (Number of characters: 97):
Suddenly she realized that her mother was the one with a
monopoly on self-interest in her family.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1821 (Number of characters: 178):
My client then realized that every time she thought of
doing something for herself, she not only felt very anxious, but also ashamed that she was acting
as ghastly as her mother.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1822 (Number of characters: 138):
Fawns need to understand that fear of being attacked for lapses in ingratiation causes them
to forfeit their boundaries, rights and needs.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1823 (Number of characters: 78):
Understanding this dynamic is a necessary but not
sufficient step in recovery.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1824 (Number of characters: 186):
There are many codependents who realize their penchant for
forfeiting themselves, but who instantly forget everything they know when self-assertion is
appropriate in their relationships.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1825 (Number of characters: 113):
In early recovery, I became increasingly aware in new dating situations of how much I was
over-eliciting my date.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1826 (Number of characters: 115):
Eventually, I was struck by how little I had to say for myself, but I found
it very difficult to break the pattern.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1827 (Number of characters: 99):
Over time, I realized that my intention to be more forthcoming was frightening me into a
flashback.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1828 (Number of characters: 67):
This made me dissociate and forget everything I had planned to say.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1829 (Number of characters: 108):
All I could think
to do, when an amygdala hijacking took my left-brain off line, was to get my date talking.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1830 (Number of characters: 82):
I then
regressed to the tried and true safety position of listening and eliciting.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1831 (Number of characters: 122):
It wasn’t until my therapist recommended that I write down some key words on my palm
that I began to master the situation.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1832 (Number of characters: 144):
Looking at the words brought my left-brain back online, and
I gradually got better at holding up my end of the getting-to-know-you conversation.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1833 (Number of characters: 90):
Much later, I had the realization: “No wonder I wind up with one narcissist after another.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1834 (Number of characters: 65):
Narcissists love me because I am so enabling of their monologing.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1835 (Number of characters: 143):
I probably met lots of nice
balanced people who did not want another date with me because it seemed like I was hiding and
hard to get to know.”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1836 (Number of characters: 72):
Around this time I also had a fawn type friend who had the same problem.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1837 (Number of characters: 167):
She liked to joke
that her listening and eliciting defenses where so perfected that she could turn anyone, even a
blank-screen therapist, into a monologing narcissist.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1838 (Number of characters: 141):
To break free of their codependence, survivors must learn to stay present to the fear that
triggers the self-abdication of the fawn response.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1839 (Number of characters: 118):
In the face of their fear, they must try on and
practice an expanding repertoire of more functional responses to fear.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1840 (Number of characters: 180):
[See the flashback
management steps in the next chapter]
Facing The Fear Of Self-Disclosure
Real motivation for surmounting this challenge usually comes from family of origin work.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1841 (Number of characters: 144):
We
need to intuit and puzzle together a detailed picture of the trauma that first frightened us out of
our instincts of healthy self-expression.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1842 (Number of characters: 162):
When we emotionally remember how overpowered we were as children, we can begin to
realize that it was because we were too small and powerless to assert ourselves.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1843 (Number of characters: 70):
But now in our
adult bodies, we are in a much more powerful situation.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1844 (Number of characters: 156):
And even though we might still momentarily feel small and helpless when we are triggered,
we can learn to remind ourselves that we are now in an adult body.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1845 (Number of characters: 140):
We have an adult status that
now offers us many more resources to champion ourselves and to effectively protest unfairness
in relationships.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1846 (Number of characters: 121):
Grieving Through Codependence
I usually find that deconstructing codependence involves a considerable amount of grieving.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1847 (Number of characters: 126):
Typically this entails many tears about the loss and pain of being so long without healthy self-
interest and self-protection.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1848 (Number of characters: 92):
Grieving also unlocks healthy anger about a life lived with such a
diminished sense of self.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1849 (Number of characters: 62):
This anger can then be used to build a healthy fight response.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1850 (Number of characters: 184):
Once again, the fight response
is the basis of the instinct of self-protection, of balanced assertiveness, and of the courage that is
needed to make relationships equal and reciprocal.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1851 (Number of characters: 153):
Later Stage Recovery
To facilitate the reclaiming of assertiveness, I encourage the survivor to imagine herself
confronting a current or past unfairness.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1852 (Number of characters: 115):
This type of role-playing is often delicate work, as it can
invoke a therapeutic flashback that brings up old fear.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1853 (Number of characters: 330):
As the survivor learns to stay present in assertiveness role-plays, she becomes more aware
of how fear triggers her into fawning. She can then practice staying present to her fear and acting
assertively anyway. With enough practice she then heals the developmental arrest of not having learned “to feel
the fear and do it anyway.”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1854 (Number of characters: 115):
This in turn sets the stage for deconstructing self-harmful reactions to
fear like giving or compromising too much.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1855 (Number of characters: 67):
Moreover, it makes the survivor more adept at
flashback management.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1856 (Number of characters: 83):
As later stage recovery progresses, the survivor increasingly “knows her own mind”.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1857 (Number of characters: 101):
She

slowly dissolves the habit of reflexively agreeing with other people’s preferences and opinions.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1858 (Number of characters: 74):
She more easily expresses her own point of view and makes her own choices.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1859 (Number of characters: 56):
And most
importantly, she learns to stay inside herself.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1860 (Number of characters: 122):
Many fawns survived by constantly focusing their awareness on their parents to figure out
what was needed to appease them.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1861 (Number of characters: 89):
Some became almost psychic in their ability to read their
parents moods and expectations.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1862 (Number of characters: 84):
This then helped them to figure out the best response to
neutralize parental danger.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1863 (Number of characters: 54):
For some, it even occasionally won them some approval.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1864 (Number of characters: 166):
Survivors now need to deconstruct this habit by working to stay more inside their own
experience without constantly projecting their attention outward to read others.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1865 (Number of characters: 107):
Fawn-types who
are still habituated to people-pleasing, must work on reducing their ingratiating behaviors.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1866 (Number of characters: 143):
I have
noticed over the years that the degree to which a survivor strains to please me reflects the degree
to which his parents were dangerous.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1867 (Number of characters: 104):
Recovering requires us to become increasingly mindful of our automatic matching and
mirroring behaviors.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1868 (Number of characters: 90):
This helps us decrease the habit of reflexively agreeing with everything that
anyone says.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1869 (Number of characters: 69):
It is a great accomplishment to significantly reduce verbal matching.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1870 (Number of characters: 82):
It is an even more
powerful achievement to reduce inauthentic emotional mirroring.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1871 (Number of characters: 199):
Dysfunctional emotional matching is seen in behaviors such as acting amused at destructive
sarcasm, acting loving when someone is punishing, and acting forgiving when someone is
repetitively hurtful.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1872 (Number of characters: 41):
I call this emotional individuation work.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1873 (Number of characters: 258):
As such, recovery involves setting the kind of
boundaries that help us to stay true to our own actual emotional experience. In advanced recovery, this occurs when we reduce the habit of automatically shifting our
mood to match someone else’s emotional state.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1874 (Number of characters: 74):
By this, I do not mean suppressing empathic
attunement when it is genuine.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1875 (Number of characters: 75):
Crying and laughing along with an intimate is a truly wonderful
experience.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1876 (Number of characters: 121):
Rather, what I am recommending here is resisting the pressure to pretend you are always
feeling the same as someone else.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1877 (Number of characters: 53):
You do not have to laugh when something is not funny.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1878 (Number of characters: 141):
When
a friend is feeling bad, you do not have to act like you feel bad. When you are feeling bad, you
do not have to act like you feel happy.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1879 (Number of characters: 69):
Thankfully, I learned a great deal about this from being a therapist.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1880 (Number of characters: 230):
When my client is
depressed, it does not help him if I adjust my mood and act depressed. Yet I am always genuinely empathic toward my client’s depression, because I can put
myself in his shoes via my own experiences of depression.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1881 (Number of characters: 95):
I can be there caringly for him
without abandoning my own feeling of contentment in the moment.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1882 (Number of characters: 176):
Similarly I can be feeling depressed while you are enthused about something and genuinely
appreciate your happiness without shamefully abandoning my temporarily depressed self.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1883 (Number of characters: 32):
Here is another example of this.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1884 (Number of characters: 86):
You are in a great mood and tell me that you loved this old
musical that you just saw.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1885 (Number of characters: 53):
I, however, am feeling down and dislike old musicals.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1886 (Number of characters: 85):
If I were a fight
type, the table would be set for a great deal of mutual alienation.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1887 (Number of characters: 191):
If I were an unrecovered fawn type, however, I might strangle my bad mood and my
musical taste, and anxiously squeeze out a high-pitched, forced frivolity about how wonderful
Fred Astaire is.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1888 (Number of characters: 55):
Instead, I can reach for a deeper more authentic truth.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1889 (Number of characters: 62):
I can let you know that I
am pleased you had such a nice time.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1890 (Number of characters: 69):
After all, I really believe in different strokes for different
folks.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1891 (Number of characters: 69):
As I write this, I flash on Bruno, a dear old hospice client of mine.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1892 (Number of characters: 83):
During a visit, with him,
he told me: “I can’t stand talking to that new volunteer.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1893 (Number of characters: 42):
She acts so morose whenever she visits
me.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1894 (Number of characters: 45):
I mean, who the hell’s dying here… me or her?

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1895 (Number of characters: 136):
I tell ya I don’t know who is worse – her or
that other smiley volunteer, the one who always acts like she just walked into Disneyland.”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1896 (Number of characters: 122):
“Disapproval Is Okay With Me”
Early in recovery, an esteemed mentor gave me the affirmation “Disapproval is okay with me.”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1897 (Number of characters: 98):
Codependently, I enthusiastically welcomed his advice that I should practice it until it was true.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1898 (Number of characters: 38):
Privately I thought “Surely you jest!”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1899 (Number of characters: 121):
I had survived the previous thirty years with a Will
Rogers-like mission to prove that “I never met a man I didn’t like.”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1900 (Number of characters: 209):
I did not yet know that I had unconsciously gravitated to this all-or-none nonsense because I
was somewhat desperately trying to seduce everyone I met into liking me in the hope that I could
finally feel safe.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1901 (Number of characters: 103):
As I thought further about this affirmation, I judged it as patently absurd and eminently
unachievable.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1902 (Number of characters: 79):
Yet within a week something ignited in me that really wanted it to become true.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1903 (Number of characters: 130):
Since it was still a long time before I knew anything about code-pendence, it took almost
two decades to make any progress at all.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1904 (Number of characters: 106):
The importance of learning to handle and accept
disapproval faded in and out of my awareness myriad times.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1905 (Number of characters: 105):
But now as I write thirty years later, I feel it is one of the most important things I have ever
learned.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1906 (Number of characters: 152):
I rest most of the time in receiving so much approval from my friends and intimates that
I can usually let in their constructive feedback fairly easily.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1907 (Number of characters: 105):
As a corollary to this, I rarely care
what people think about me who I do not know or who do not know me.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1908 (Number of characters: 53):
And, of course, this is not a perfect accomplishment.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1909 (Number of characters: 62):
Disapproval can still on occasion
trigger me into a flashback.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1910 (Number of characters: 171):
But it delights me to report that I now experience most disapproval
with considerable equanimity. I even occasionally experience some people’s disapproval as a
good thing.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1911 (Number of characters: 97):
Sometimes it is a validation that I am doing the right thing and evolving in the right
direction.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1912 (Number of characters: 150):
Nowhere is this truer than with the disapproval of the narcissistic parents or partners of
clients whom I am working to rescue from their enslavement.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1913 (Number of characters: 147):
Their disapproval of me is actually
an affirmation that I have indeed been involved in right action. Most of the time, disapproval is okay with me.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1914 (Number of characters: 342):
MANAGING EMOTIONAL FLASHBACKS
Emotional flashbacks are intensely disturbing regressions [“amygdala hijackings”] to the
overwhelming feeling-states of your childhood abandonment. When you are stuck in a flashback,
fear, shame and/or depression can dominate your experience. These are some common experiences of being in an emotional flashback.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1915 (Number of characters: 38):
You feel little,
fragile and helpless.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1916 (Number of characters: 26):
Everything feels too hard.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1917 (Number of characters: 18):
Life is too scary.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1918 (Number of characters: 43):
Being seen feels excruciatingly
vulnerable.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1919 (Number of characters: 30):
Your battery seems to be dead.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1920 (Number of characters: 80):
In the worst flashbacks an apocalypse feels like it will
imminently be upon you.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1921 (Number of characters: 97):
When you are trapped in a flashback, you are reliving the worst emotional times of you
childhood.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1922 (Number of characters: 124):
Everything feels overwhelming and confusing, especially because there are rarely any
visual components to a Cptsd flashback.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1923 (Number of characters: 159):
This is because, as Goleman’s work shows, amygdala
hijackings are intense reactions in the emotional memory part of the brain that override the
rational brain.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1924 (Number of characters: 159):
These reactions occur in the brains of people who have been triggered into a 4F
reaction so often, that minor events can now trigger them into a panicky state.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1925 (Number of characters: 134):
This is a list of 13 practical steps for helping yourself to manage an emotional flashback:
13 Steps for Managing Emotional Flashbacks

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1926 (Number of characters: 49):
[Focus on Bold Print when flashback is active]
1.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1927 (Number of characters: 43):
Say to yourself: “I am having a flashback”.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1928 (Number of characters: 138):
Flashbacks take you into a timeless part
of the psyche that feels as helpless, hopeless and surrounded by danger as you were in
childhood.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1929 (Number of characters: 92):
The feelings and sensations you are experiencing are past memories that
cannot hurt you now.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1930 (Number of characters: 58):
2. Remind yourself: “I feel afraid but I am not in danger!

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1931 (Number of characters: 120):
I am safe now, here in the
present.” Remember you are now in the safety of the present, far from the danger of
the past.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1932 (Number of characters: 42):
3. Own your right/need to have boundaries.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1933 (Number of characters: 141):
Remind yourself that you do not have to
allow anyone to mistreat you; you are free to leave dangerous situations and protest
unfair behavior.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1934 (Number of characters: 41):
4. Speak reassuringly to the Inner Child.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1935 (Number of characters: 148):
The child needs to know that you love
her/him unconditionally– that s/he can come to you for comfort and protection when
s/he feels lost and scared.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1936 (Number of characters: 2):
5.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1937 (Number of characters: 30):
Deconstruct eternity thinking.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1938 (Number of characters: 82):
In childhood, fear and abandonment felt endless – a
safer future was unimaginable.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1939 (Number of characters: 58):
Remember this flashback will pass as it always has
before.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1940 (Number of characters: 128):
6. Remind yourself that you are in an adult body with allies, skills and resources to
protect you that you never had as a child.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1941 (Number of characters: 56):
[Feeling small and fragile is a sign of a
flashback.]
7.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1942 (Number of characters: 25):
Ease back into your body.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1943 (Number of characters: 68):
Fear launches you into “heady” worrying, or numbing and
spacing out.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1944 (Number of characters: 108):
[a] Gently ask your body to Relax: feel each of your major muscle groups and softly
encourage them to relax.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1945 (Number of characters: 60):
[Tightened muscles send false danger signals to your
brain.]

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1946 (Number of characters: 30):
[b] Breathe deeply and slowly.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1947 (Number of characters: 42):
[Holding your breath also signals danger.]

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1948 (Number of characters: 67):
[c] Slow down: rushing presses your brain’s flight response button.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1949 (Number of characters: 177):
[d] Find a safe place to unwind and soothe yourself: wrap yourself in a blanket, hold
a pillow or a stuffed animal, lie down on your bed or in a closet or in a bath;
take a nap.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1950 (Number of characters: 91):
[e] Feel the fear in your body without reacting to it. Fear is just an energy in your
body.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1951 (Number of characters: 45):
It cannot hurt you if you do not run from it.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1952 (Number of characters: 62):
8. Resist the Inner Critic’s Drasticizing and Catastrophizing.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1953 (Number of characters: 135):
[a] Use Thought-stopping to halt the critic’s endless exaggerations of danger, and its
constant planning to control the uncontrollable.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1954 (Number of characters: 42):
Refuse to shame, hate or
abandon yourself.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1955 (Number of characters: 90):
Channel the anger of self- attack into saying “NO” to your
critic’s unfair self-criticism.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1956 (Number of characters: 139):
[b] Use Thought-substitution & Thought-correction to replace negative thinking
with a memorized list of your qualities and accomplishments.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1957 (Number of characters: 28):
9. Allow yourself to grieve.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1958 (Number of characters: 97):
Flashbacks are opportunities to release old, unexpressed
feelings of fear, hurt, and abandonment.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1959 (Number of characters: 82):
Validate and soothe your child’s past
experience of helplessness and hopelessness.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1960 (Number of characters: 94):
Healthy grieving can turn your tears into
self-compassion and your anger into self-protection.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1961 (Number of characters: 50):
10. Cultivate safe relationships and seek support.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1962 (Number of characters: 66):
Take time alone when you need it, but
don’t let shame isolate you.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1963 (Number of characters: 44):
Feeling shame doesn’t mean you are shameful.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1964 (Number of characters: 101):
Educate
your intimates about flashbacks and ask them to help you talk and feel your way
through them.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1965 (Number of characters: 3):
11.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1966 (Number of characters: 64):
Learn to identify the types of triggers that lead to flashbacks.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1967 (Number of characters: 72):
Avoid unsafe people,
places, activities and triggering mental processes.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1968 (Number of characters: 97):
Practice preventive maintenance
with these steps when triggering situations are unavoidable.

12.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1969 (Number of characters: 41):
Figure out what you are flashing back to.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1970 (Number of characters: 104):
Flashbacks are opportunities to discover,
validate and heal your wounds from past abuse and abandonment.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1971 (Number of characters: 108):
They also point to
your still unmet developmental needs and can provide you with motivation to get them
met.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1972 (Number of characters: 3):
13.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1973 (Number of characters: 40):
Be patient with a slow recovery process.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1974 (Number of characters: 166):
It takes time in the present to become de-
adrenalized, and considerable time in the future to gradually decrease the intensity,
duration and frequency of flashbacks.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1975 (Number of characters: 48):
Real recovery is a gradually progressive process

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1976 (Number of characters: 76):
[often two steps forward, one step back], not an attained salvation fantasy.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1977 (Number of characters: 46):
Don’t beat
yourself up for having a flashback.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1978 (Number of characters: 134):
My clients, who post this somewhere conspicuous until they memorize the gist of it, typically
progress more rapidly in their recovery.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1979 (Number of characters: 92):
You can easily print out a copy from the “13 Steps” page
of my website: www.pete-walker.com.

chunk 1980 (Number of characters: 126):
Triggers And Emotional Flashbacks
A trigger is an external or internal stimulus that activates us into an emotional flashback.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1981 (Number of characters: 87):
This
often occurs on a subliminal level outside the boundaries of normal consciousness.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1982 (Number of characters: 434):
Recognizing
what triggers us can therefore be difficult. Nonetheless, becoming increasingly mindful of our
triggers is crucial because it sometimes allows us to avoid flashback-inducing people, situations
and behaviors. External triggers are people, places, things, events, facial expressions, styles of
communication, etc., that remind us of our original trauma in a way that flashes us back into the
painful feelings of those times.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1983 (Number of characters: 264):
Here are some examples of powerful and common triggers:
revisiting your parents; seeing someone who resembles a childhood abuser; experiencing the
anniversary of an especially traumatic event; hearing someone use a parent’s shaming tone of
voice or turn of phrase.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1984 (Number of characters: 42):
Many triggers however are not so explicit.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1985 (Number of characters: 114):
Sometimes all unknown adults can trigger us
into fear even when there is no resemblance to our original abuser[s].

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1986 (Number of characters: 148):
I still occasionally feel
triggered when I come across a group of teenagers, because I grew up in a neighborhood where
there were many violent ones.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1987 (Number of characters: 104):
For this reason, my son who is quite empathic joked that he will
not become a teenager when he grows up.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1988 (Number of characters: 95):
Sometimes someone looking at us, or even noticing us, can trigger us into fear and toxic
shame.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1989 (Number of characters: 84):
One of my clients once came in intensely triggered because a cat was staring at him.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1990 (Number of characters: 114):
Other common triggers include making a mistake, asking for help, or having to speak in front of
a group of people.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1991 (Number of characters: 89):
Moreover, simply feeling tired, sick, lonely or hungry can sometimes trigger a
flashback.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1992 (Number of characters: 48):
Any type of physical pain can also be a trigger.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1993 (Number of characters: 64):
For many survivors, authority figures are the ultimate triggers.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1994 (Number of characters: 159):
I have known several
survivors, who have never gotten so much as a parking ticket, who cringe in anxiety whenever
they come across a policeman or a police car.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1995 (Number of characters: 77):
It took me decades to overcome my intense performance anxiety about teaching.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1996 (Number of characters: 67):
Nothing
could trigger me more than an upcoming teaching engagement.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1997 (Number of characters: 109):
Fortunately I was unwilling to
give up this activity, because I enjoyed it most of the time once I got going.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1998 (Number of characters: 178):
I did not make any
real progress, beyond learning to “white-knuckle it,” until I recognized my performance anxiety

as a flashback to the danger of talking at family dinner time.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 1999 (Number of characters: 134):
This recognition allowed me to see
that I was unconsciously terrified that my parents were going to show up and scoff at me in
public.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2000 (Number of characters: 239):
At first, I [my critic] labeled this a preposterous idea, but when I started to imagine and
practice aggressively defending myself against them on the drive to my teaching engagement, I
soon experienced an enormous reduction in my anxiety.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2001 (Number of characters: 185):
This experience lead to me formulating
Step 6 in the flashback management steps above, as well as my ideas about angering against the
critic, which will be explored in the next chapter.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2002 (Number of characters: 203):
The Look: A Common Trigger Of Emotional Flashbacks
Early on in working with this model, I was surprised that certain clients with relatively moderate
childhood abuse were plagued by emotional flashbacks.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2003 (Number of characters: 71):
Most of them in fact were quite sure that
they had never even been hit.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2004 (Number of characters: 94):
Many of them however would talk about how they hated it when
their parents gave them the look.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2005 (Number of characters: 193):
The look, in most cases, is the facial expression that typically accompanies contempt. Contempt is a powerful punishing visage backed up by an emotional force-field of intimidation
and disgust.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2006 (Number of characters: 75):
A raised voice can be added intermittently to the look to amp up its power.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2007 (Number of characters: 158):
When a parent gives the look to a child, she is “telling” him that he is not only in serious
jeopardy, but that he is also “a sorry excuse for a human being”.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2008 (Number of characters: 301):
Over time, the look can make
the recipient feel terrified and repugnant, as it drives him into an emotional flashback of fear and
shame. When the look is used to control an older child, it commonly flashes her back to an earlier,
pre-memory time when the look was empowered by traumatizing punishment.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2009 (Number of characters: 290):
The look rarely
terrifies a child into obedience unless it has previously been paired with hitting or other dire
consequences. Years of working at Parental Stress Services convinced me that most young
children ignore the look unless it was previously accompanied by traumatizing punishment.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2010 (Number of characters: 81):
Typically, the look is empowered via a psychological process called conditioning.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2011 (Number of characters: 51):
Here is a
classic example of aversive conditioning.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2012 (Number of characters: 100):
Technicians deliver an electric shock to an animal in a
cage at the same time that they ring a bell.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2013 (Number of characters: 222):
The animal of course has a fearful and distressed
response to the shock. It does not, however, take many repetitions of pairing the sound with the
shock before the sound alone elicits the same upset response in the animal.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2014 (Number of characters: 80):
This is analogous, I believe, to how a child learns to be terrified of the look.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2015 (Number of characters: 169):
With enough
pairings of the look with physical punishment or extreme abandonment, the parent can
eventually delete the smack and get the same results with just the look.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2016 (Number of characters: 141):
With enough repetitions
in early childhood, this pairing can last a lifetime, so that the parent can control the child forever
with the look.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2017 (Number of characters: 137):
In my hospice work, I have seen several dying, ninety-pound mothers still able to
put the fear of god into their huge sons with the look.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2018 (Number of characters: 121):
The look then is a powerful trigger for making adult survivors flashback to the fear and
humiliation of their childhoods.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2019 (Number of characters: 196):
Once again, many of my clients do not remember this because
the punishment only had to accompany the look for a few months while they were toddlers
before it became permanently wired as a trigger.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2020 (Number of characters: 88):
Moreover, it is the rare person who has any
memory before he is three or four years old.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2021 (Number of characters: 71):
Unfortunately the look can continue to work even after the parent dies.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2022 (Number of characters: 41):
There are at least

two reasons for this.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2023 (Number of characters: 152):
First, we internalize our parents in a way that they can subliminally appear in our
imaginations and give us the look whenever we are less than perfect.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2024 (Number of characters: 60):
This includes
“imperfections” in thought, feeling or action.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2025 (Number of characters: 131):
Sadly, I often see this subliminal look mimicked on the faces of my flashbacked clients as
they scowl contemptuously at themselves.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2026 (Number of characters: 117):
And second, when anyone else looks at us disapprovingly, we can generalize that they are
as dangerous as our parents.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2027 (Number of characters: 85):
This is what happened to me in the emotional flashback that I
described in chapter 1.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2028 (Number of characters: 178):
The worst thing about having been traumatized with the look in childhood is that we can
erroneously transfer and project our memory of it onto other people when we are triggered.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2029 (Number of characters: 139):
We
are especially prone to doing this with authority figures or people that resemble our parents, even
when they are not sporting the look.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2030 (Number of characters: 148):
Internal vs. External Triggers
As we move out of early recovery, we begin to observe that internal triggers are even more
common than external ones.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2031 (Number of characters: 63):
Such triggers are commonly the nasty spawn of the inner critic.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2032 (Number of characters: 93):
Typically they are thoughts and visualizations about endangerment or the need for perfection.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2033 (Number of characters: 76):
The survivor may, seemingly without reason, visualize someone being abusive.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2034 (Number of characters: 258):
Moreover he can
also, seemingly out of the blue, worry himself into a flashback by simply thinking he is not
perfectly executing a task that he is undertaking. He can also frighten himself by enumerating the
many ways that he might mess up any upcoming task.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2035 (Number of characters: 129):
When internal triggering is at its worst, small potato miscues and peccadilloes trigger us
into a full blown emotional flashback.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2036 (Number of characters: 117):
We then devolve into a polarized process of negative-
noticing – an incessant preoccupation with defects and hazards.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2037 (Number of characters: 64):
We perseverate about everything
that has gone or could go wrong.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2038 (Number of characters: 166):
As recovery progresses, many survivors are shocked to discover that the majority of their
flashbacks are triggered internally by these types of inner critic programs.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2039 (Number of characters: 132):
We will explore below
and in the next two chapters the ways we can rescue ourselves from the critic’s internal
triggering processes.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2040 (Number of characters: 225):
Progressive Trigger-Recognition
With ongoing recovery, we become more knowledgeable about our triggers, and avoid them
when practical. Identifying our triggers also helps us to get into flashback management mode
more quickly.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2041 (Number of characters: 72):
Recognition further aids us to handle unavoidable triggering situations.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2042 (Number of characters: 161):
Forethought allows
us to prophylactically practice flashback management before we get activated, as I did in the
performance anxiety situation mentioned earlier.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2043 (Number of characters: 96):
Recognizing the moment of triggering is even more important than recognizing the trigger
itself.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2044 (Number of characters: 354):
This is because flashbacks sometimes start out subtly and then progressively become more
intense. Early recognition therefore helps us to invoke the steps earlier, and decrease the intensity
and duration of the flashback. Finally, resolving a flashback requires rebalancing significant biochemical changes in the
brain and body that take time to subside.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2045 (Number of characters: 152):
For example, over-adrenalization sometimes
dramatically morphs into the hangover of adrenalin exhaustion, before the adrenal function can
be rebalanced.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2046 (Number of characters: 125):
Decreasing the intensity of a flashback with quick remedial action decreases the
time it takes for our physiology to recover.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2047 (Number of characters: 460):
Signs Of Being In A Flashback
We can often find ourselves in a flashback without ever having seen the “flash.” There are a
variety of clues that we can learn to identify as signs that we are caught in a flashback. This is
essential to recovery, as naming our experience “flashback” [step#1 in flashback management]
often immediately brings some relief. Even more importantly, it points us in the direction of
working the other 12 steps of flashback management.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2048 (Number of characters: 84):
One common sign of being flashed-back is that we feel small, helpless, and hopeless.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2049 (Number of characters: 115):
In
intense flashbacks this magnifies into feeling so ashamed that we are loath to go out or show our
face anywhere.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2050 (Number of characters: 82):
Feeling fragile, on edge, delicate and easily crushable is another aspect of this.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2051 (Number of characters: 101):
The survivor may also notice an evaporation of whatever self-esteem he has earned since he left
home.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2052 (Number of characters: 103):
This is a flashback to the childhood years where implicit family rules forbade any self-
esteem at all.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2053 (Number of characters: 107):
Another common clue that we are flashing back is an increase in the virulence of the inner
or outer critic.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2054 (Number of characters: 136):
This typically looks like increased drasticizing and catastrophizing, as well as
intensified self-criticism or judgmentalness of others.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2055 (Number of characters: 161):
A very common example of this is lapsing
into extremely polarized, all-or-none thinking such as only being able to see what is wrong with
yourself and/or others.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2056 (Number of characters: 174):
In my own mid-level recovery, I learned that when I was feeling especially judgmental of
others, it usually meant that I had flashed back to being around my critical parents.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2057 (Number of characters: 74):
The trigger
was usually that some vulnerability of mine was in ascendancy.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2058 (Number of characters: 159):
In response, I then over-noticed
others’ faults so that I could justify avoiding them and the embarrassment of being seen in a state
of not being shiny enough.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2059 (Number of characters: 140):
Another clue that we are in a flashback occurs when we notice that our emotional reactions
are out of proportion to what has triggered them.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2060 (Number of characters: 38):
Here are two common instances of this:

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2061 (Number of characters: 99):
[1] a
minor upset feels like an emergency; [2] a minor unfairness feels like a travesty of justice.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2062 (Number of characters: 132):
In the first instance, you drop a book that you are carrying and launch into an angry, self-
berating tirade that lingers for hours.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2063 (Number of characters: 167):
In the second instance, another driver’s relatively harmless,
un-signaled lane change triggers you into rageful indignation that reverberates in your psyche for
hours.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2064 (Number of characters: 172):
When we are not mindful at such times, we can erupt against ourselves in self-disgust and
self-hatred, or we can unfairly explode out against the relatively innocent other.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2065 (Number of characters: 287):
On the other hand, we can choose healthy flashback management once we recognize these
examples as flashbacks to the real emergencies and injustices of our childhood. Furthermore, we
can harvest recovery out of these unpleasant flashbacks by seeing them as proof that we were
traumatized.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2066 (Number of characters: 130):
When we do the latter, we can morph our anger into healthy indignation about the
outrageously unfair conditions of our upbringing.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2067 (Number of characters: 122):
More On Self-Medication
Another clue about being in a flashback is an increased use of primitive self-soothing techniques.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2068 (Number of characters: 130):
Many survivors learn early in life to manage their painful feelings with food, distracting
activities or mood-altering substances.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2069 (Number of characters: 238):
Over time self-medication can become habitual and
devolve into substance or process addictions. With self-medication, I believe there is a continuum of severity that stretches from
occasional use on one end to true addiction on the other.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2070 (Number of characters: 58):
For many survivors, self-medication is a
matter of degree.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2071 (Number of characters: 253):
An especially strong urge to use more substance or process than normal is a
powerful clue that you are in a flashback. With practice, mindfully noticing a sudden upsurge in
craving can be interpreted as the need to invoke the flashback management steps.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2072 (Number of characters: 112):
Moreover, I
see many survivors gradually decrease their self-medicating habits by effectively using these
steps.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2073 (Number of characters: 30):
Flashbacks In Therapy Sessions

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2074 (Number of characters: 78):
This section is for survivors who are in therapy or contemplating starting it.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2075 (Number of characters: 277):
Over the years, I
have noticed that as survivors feel safe enough with me, opportunities arise more frequently for
working with flashbacks during sessions. Sometimes it even seems that some part of them
“schedules” their flashbacks to occur just prior to or during our session.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2076 (Number of characters: 79):
It is as if they are
looking for “on the job training” in flashback management.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2077 (Number of characters: 87):
Some therapists describe this as
regression in the service of building the healthy ego.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2078 (Number of characters: 115):
I recently experienced this with a client who rushed into my office five minutes late, visibly
flushed and anxious.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2079 (Number of characters: 56):
She opened the session by exclaiming: “I’m such a loser.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2080 (Number of characters: 26):
I can’t do
anything right.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2081 (Number of characters: 37):
You must be sick of working with me.”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2082 (Number of characters: 120):
This was someone who had, on previous
occasions, been moved by my validation of her ongoing accomplishments in our work.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2083 (Number of characters: 172):
Based on what she had uncovered about her mother’s punitive perfectionism in previous
sessions, I was certain that being late had triggered her into an emotional flashback.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2084 (Number of characters: 116):
In this
moment, she was experiencing right-brain emotional dominance and a decrease in left-brain
rational thinking.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2085 (Number of characters: 115):
As so often happens in a flashback, she temporarily lost access to her post-
childhood knowledge and understanding.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2086 (Number of characters: 141):
This appears to be a mechanism of dissociation, and in
this instance, it rendered my client amnesiac of my high regard for our work together.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2087 (Number of characters: 161):
I believe this type of dissociation also accounts for the recurring disappearance of
previously established trust that commonly occurs with emotional flashbacks.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2088 (Number of characters: 268):

chunk 2118 (Number of characters: 184):
Managing The Inner Critic [Step # 8]
When Cptsd survivors come of age and launch from the traumatizing family, they are often
unaware that their minds are dominated by an inner critic.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2119 (Number of characters: 170):
In assisting others to manage
flashbacks, the most common help I offer is to encourage them to challenge the alarmist and
perfectionistic programming of the inner critic.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2120 (Number of characters: 55):
This type of scenario arises frequently in my practice.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2121 (Number of characters: 110):
A client, in the midst of reporting an
inconsequential mistake, suddenly launches into a catastrophizing tale.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2122 (Number of characters: 117):
He reports from his
critic’s nightmarish fantasy that his life is deteriorating into a cascading series of disasters.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2123 (Number of characters: 78):
He is
flashing back to the way he was continuously over-punished in childhood.

--------------------------------------------------

came back from my bathroom break this morning

chunk 2131 (Number of characters: 143):

chunk 2137 (Number of characters: 343):
The inner critic not only exacerbates flashbacks, but eventually grows into a psychic agency
that triggers them. Reversing the damaging effects of the critic is the subject of the next two
chapters
ADVANCED FLASHBACK MANAGEMENT
Waking Up In The Abandonment Depression
As recovery progresses, you notice more subtlety in the triggering process.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2138 (Number of characters: 82):
As you do, you
become more mindful of your inner critic’s hard-to-detect triggers.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2139 (Number of characters: 55):
You also discover that some
triggers are indiscernible.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2140 (Number of characters: 63):
This is especially true of triggering that occurs during sleep.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2141 (Number of characters: 177):
Advanced flashback management, then, involves learning how to manage the disconcerting
experience of falling asleep feeling reasonably put together and waking up in a flashback.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2142 (Number of characters: 257):
Typically this occurs because a dream has triggered you into a flashback. If you remember the
dream, you can sometimes figure out why it triggered you. With growing mindfulness you may
even understand which events from the previous day triggered your dream.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2143 (Number of characters: 77):
The most difficult situation to manage is when you cannot remember the dream.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2144 (Number of characters: 69):
This type
of flashback can feel particularly unfair and discouraging.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2145 (Number of characters: 129):
It is rich fodder for the critic, which
can declare that you are not only getting nowhere in his recovery, you are getting worse.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2146 (Number of characters: 36):
Flashbacks As The Inner Child’s Plea

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2147 (Number of characters: 135):
For Help
With undetectable triggers, I find that it is most helpful to see a flashback as a communication
from the child that you were.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2148 (Number of characters: 112):
The child is reminding you that he woke up feeling desolate
innumerable times in that house that was not a home.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2149 (Number of characters: 103):
He woke up daunted by the prospect of
once again having to reenter the poisonous milieu of your family.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2150 (Number of characters: 124):
The child is now asking you to
meet his unmet need of having someone to go to for comfort when he wakes up feeling
wretched.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2151 (Number of characters: 31):
It is as if he is saying: “See!

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2152 (Number of characters: 225):
This is how bad it was – this is how overwhelmed,
ashamed and miserable I felt so much of the time.”
Managing the pain of waking up in the abandonment depression is one of the most difficult,
long term challenges in recovery.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2153 (Number of characters: 243):
Sleep seems to be a regressive, right-brain dominant
experience. It is not uncommon to wake up with a temporary loss of access to the left-brain
cognitive functions that control our more sophisticated understandings of our present-day reality.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2154 (Number of characters: 111):
Without the latter, flashback management often reverts to the critic and our early childhood

attempts to cope.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2155 (Number of characters: 134):
This creates a fruitful ground for the critic to explode its arsenal of self-
pathologizing programs [enumerated in the next chapter].

chunk 2164 (Number of characters: 148):

--------------------------------------------------

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2169 (Number of characters: 138):
For me, this is now my most practiced flashback management
process: breaking my merge with the critic to concentrate on caring for myself.

--------------------------------------------------
