MINDFULNESS AND SHRINKING THE OUTER CRITIC
Reducing outer critic reactivity requires a great deal of mindfulness.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2886 (Number of characters: 173):
This is as essential for fight
types who act out aggressively, as it is for those trauma types who internally rant against the
entire human group known as “F*cking People!”.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2887 (Number of characters: 128):
It is also of great importance for any survivor
who is locked into alienation because of the judgmentalness of his outer critic.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2888 (Number of characters: 165):
Mindfulness, once again, is the process of becoming intricately aware of everything that is
going on inside us, especially thoughts, images, feelings and sensations.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2889 (Number of characters: 251):
In terms of outer critic
work, it is essential that we become more mindful of both the cognitive and emotional content of
our thoughts. This is the same as in inner critic work, where the two key fronts of critic shrinking are
cognitive and emotional.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2890 (Number of characters: 137):
Cognitive work in both cases involves the demolition and rebuilding
processes of thought-stopping and thought substitution, respectively.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2891 (Number of characters: 52):
And, emotional work in
both instances is grief work.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2892 (Number of characters: 125):
It is removing the critic’s fuel supply - the unexpressed childhood
anger and the uncried tears of a lifetime of abandonment.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2893 (Number of characters: 160):
When Mindfulness Appears To Intensify The Critic
In early recovery the outer critic unfortunately seems to become nastier and stronger the more we
challenge it.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2894 (Number of characters: 103):
We may even think we are counter-productively stirring it up or making it worse by
daring to resist it.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2895 (Number of characters: 149):
When mindfulness of the critic seems to strengthen it, we are typically flashing back to how
our parents rebuked our early protests at their attacks.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2896 (Number of characters: 148):
This is often impossible to remember
because our dysfunctional parents typically kill our protest function before our memory function
comes on line.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2897 (Number of characters: 129):
Nonetheless, fear of parental reprisal is often the unconscious dynamic that scares
us out of challenging our own toxic thinking.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2898 (Number of characters: 133):
This is why survivors in early recovery often need
to invoke the instinct of angry self-protection to empower their thought-stopping.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2899 (Number of characters: 102):
There is another dynamic occurring when critic-work seems to strengthen rather than
weaken the critic.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2900 (Number of characters: 128):
As we become less dissociated, we begin to notice critic processes that were
there all along under the horizon of our awareness.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2901 (Number of characters: 96):
Our childhood survival was aided by learning

to dissociate from these painful critic processes.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2902 (Number of characters: 82):
Consequently, many of us come into recovery
barely able to even notice the critic.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2903 (Number of characters: 86):
Our recovering depends on us using mindfulness to decrease our habits of dissociation.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2904 (Number of characters: 110):
Only then can we see the critic programs that we need to deconstruct, shrink and consciously
disidentify from.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2905 (Number of characters: 121):
This typically involves learning to tolerate the pain that comes from
discovering how pervasive and strong the critic is.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2906 (Number of characters: 110):
This pain is sometimes a hard pill to swallow
because progress in fighting the critic is hard to see at first.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2907 (Number of characters: 109):
And then, even when our shrinking
work is effective, progress usually feels disappointingly slow and gradual.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2908 (Number of characters: 93):
This is especially true
during a flashback, when the critic can seem to be as strong as ever.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2909 (Number of characters: 65):
As stated earlier, the critic grew carcinogenically in childhood.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2910 (Number of characters: 84):
It is like a pervasive cancer
that requires many uncomfortable operations to remove.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2911 (Number of characters: 166):
Nonetheless, we can choose to face the
acute pain of critic-shrinking work because we want to end the chronic pain of having the critic
destroy our enjoyment of life.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2912 (Number of characters: 30):
It is the fight of a lifetime.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2913 (Number of characters: 430):
Thought Substitution And Correction: Supplanting the Critic
Many of us are still developmentally arrested in our need to orient our psyches towards noticing
what is good, trustworthy and loveable about others and life in general. In working to shrink the
outer critic, thought substitution is the practice of invoking positive thoughts and images of
others to help erode the critic’s intimacy-spoiling habit of picking them apart.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2914 (Number of characters: 152):
One helpful thought substitution exercise is to list five recollections of positive interactions
with a given friend, as well as five of her attributes.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2915 (Number of characters: 117):
This same technique works well when we
self-apply it to help us separate from our inner critic’s negative self-image.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2916 (Number of characters: 124):
Toolbox 5, in chapter
16, contains a written exercise to broaden your appreciation of select others who have benefitted
you.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2917 (Number of characters: 159):
Since thoughts typically give rise to speech, I also recommend that you practice the “5
positives to 1 negative” guideline when giving feedback to a loved one.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2918 (Number of characters: 115):
John Gottman’s research
has shown that this ratio is characteristic of how intimacy-successful couples communicate.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2919 (Number of characters: 122):
This
is also key because the outer critic was spawned in childhood by parental modeling that at least
reversed this ratio.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2920 (Number of characters: 246):
GRIEVING SHORTCIRCUITS THE OUTER CRITIC
The role of grieving in shrinking the outer critic is as crucial as it is with the inner critic. As with
the inner critic, angering at the outer critic helps to silence it, and crying helps to evaporate it.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2921 (Number of characters: 70):
We can use the anger of our grief to energize our thought corrections.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2922 (Number of characters: 120):
This helps us to
challenge the critic’s entrenched all-or-none perspective that everyone is as dangerous as our
parents.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2923 (Number of characters: 138):
Moreover, when our grieving opens into crying, it can release the fear that the outer
critic uses to frighten us out of opening to others.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2924 (Number of characters: 91):
Tears can also help us realize that our
loneliness is now causing us much unnecessary pain.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2925 (Number of characters: 84):
This in turn can motivate us to open to the
possibility of finding safe connections.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2926 (Number of characters: 69):
Defueling The Outer Critic Via Working The Transference

Transference

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2927 (Number of characters: 112):
[AKA projection; AKA displacement] occurs when unprocessed feelings from the
past amplify present time feelings.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2928 (Number of characters: 135):
A key characteristic of outer critic-dominated flashbacks is
that we displace emotional pain from past relationships onto current ones.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2929 (Number of characters: 137):
Transference is the
pipeline from the past that supplies the critic with anger to control, attack or disapprove of
present relationships.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2930 (Number of characters: 193):
As a baby thrives on love, so does the outer critic thrive on anger. Like a parasite, the outer
critic gorges on repressed anger, and then erroneously assigns it to present-day disappointments.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2931 (Number of characters: 165):
The most common transferrential dynamic that I witness occurs when leftover hurt about a
parent gets displaced onto someone we perceive as hurting us in the present.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2932 (Number of characters: 114):
When this occurs,
we respond to them with a magnified anger or anguish that is out of proportion to what they did.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2933 (Number of characters: 123):
Transference can also grossly distort our perceptions, and sometimes we can misperceive a
harmless person as being hurtful.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2934 (Number of characters: 154):
Transference can fire up the critic to imagine slights that do not
actually occur. Transference typically runs wild when the outer critic is on a rampage.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2935 (Number of characters: 130):
Just as the inner critic transmutes unreleased anger into self-hate, the outer critic uses it to
control and /or push others away.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2936 (Number of characters: 241):
Unexpressed and unworked through anger about childhood
hurt is a hidden reserve that the critic can always tap into. The anger work of grieving the losses
of childhood is so essential because it breaks the critic’s supply line to this anger.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2937 (Number of characters: 134):
Grieving out old unexpressed pain about our poor parenting gradually deconstructs the
process of transferring it unfairly onto others.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2938 (Number of characters: 120):
This is crucial because love and intimacy are
murdered when the critic habitually projects old anger out at an intimate.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2939 (Number of characters: 129):
Healthy Outer Critic Venting
There are times when venting from the outer critic perspective is healthy, self-protective
behavior.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2940 (Number of characters: 52):
Sometimes the outer critic’s judgments are accurate.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2941 (Number of characters: 73):
Sometimes people are acting as
abusively as our parents did in childhood.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2942 (Number of characters: 80):
In this vein, there are two healthy applications of outer
critic aggressiveness.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2943 (Number of characters: 66):
One is to protect ourselves when someone is actually attacking us.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2944 (Number of characters: 61):
The
other is in the work of grieving the losses of childhood.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 2945 (Number of characters: 135):
As we shall see in the next chapter,
survivors benefit immeasurably from angrily judging their parents’ atrocious abuse and
negligence.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3005 (Number of characters: 569):
In the next chapter, we will explore more deeply the processes of

grieving through the old hurts that we discover when we get to the bottom of our flashbacks. GRIEVING
Grieving is an irreplaceable tool for resolving the overwhelming feelings that arise during
emotional flashbacks. Moreover, grieving is the key process for working through the host of
losses that come from growing up in a Cptsd-inducing family. We grieve the losses of childhood because these losses are like deaths of important parts of
ourselves. Effective grieving brings these parts back to life.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3006 (Number of characters: 147):
In this chapter we describe the
healing that is available through the four practices of grieving: angering, crying, verbal
ventilating and feeling.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3007 (Number of characters: 189):

your recovery work my need to focus more on deconstructing and shrinking your inner critic.

“And you’ve ruined my business
How can I sell sorrow, when you know its blessing?”

chunk 3021 (Number of characters: 205):

As our capacity to grieve evolves, we typically uncover a great deal of unresolved grief about the
deadening absence of the nurturance we needed to develop and thrive.

more nurturingly.

GRIEVING AMELIORATES FLASHBACKS
“Pain is excess energy crying out for release.”

chunk 3061 (Number of characters: 240):

Grieving sometimes seems sacramental to me in its ability to move me out of the abandonment
mélange, that extremely painful and upsetting amalgam of fear, shame and depression that is at
the emotional core of most flashbacks.

chunk 3070 (Number of characters: 222):
Inner Critic Hindrances To Grieving

The greatest hindrance to effective grieving is typically the inner critic. When the critic is
especially toxic, grieving may be counterproductive and contraindicated in early recovery.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3071 (Number of characters: 161):
Those
who were repeatedly pathologized and punished for emoting in childhood may experience
grieving as exacerbating their flashbacks rather than relieving them.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3072 (Number of characters: 181):
I have worked with numerous survivors whose tears immediately triggered them into toxic
shame. Their own potentially soothing tears elicited terrible self-attacks: “I’m so pathetic!

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3073 (Number of characters: 31):
No
wonder nobody can stand me!”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3074 (Number of characters: 48):
“God, I’m so unlovable when I snivel like this!”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3075 (Number of characters: 70):
“I f*ck up, and
then make myself more of a loser by whining about it!”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3076 (Number of characters: 62):
“What good is crying for yourself – it
only makes you weaker!”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3077 (Number of characters: 175):
This latter response is particularly ironic, for once grieving is protected from the critic,
nothing can restore a person’s inner strength and coping capacity like a good cry.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3078 (Number of characters: 106):
I have defused
active suicidality on dozens of occasions by simply eliciting the suffering person’s tears.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3079 (Number of characters: 68):
Angering can also immediately trigger the survivor into toxic shame.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3080 (Number of characters: 79):
This is often true of
instances when there is only an angry thought or fantasy.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3081 (Number of characters: 88):
Dysfunctional parents typically reserve

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3082 (Number of characters: 41):

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3083 (Number of characters: 405):
Critic management is often the primary work of early stage grief work. This work involves
recognizing and challenging the ways the critic is blocking or shaming the processes of grieving. As disidentification from the critic increases, grieving can then best be initiated with low
intensity verbal ventilation. Over time verbal ventilation can be allowed to gradually increase in
sad and angry intonation.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3084 (Number of characters: 197):
Once the critic has been sufficiently diminished and once thought-correction techniques
have made the psyche more user-friendly, a person begins to tap into grief’s sweet relief-granting
potential.

chunk 3085 (Number of characters: 186):
He learns to grieve in a way that promotes and enhances compassion for the abandoned
child he was and for the survivor he is today – still struggling in the throes of painful flashbacks.

Defueling The Critic Through Grieving

chunk 3090 (Number of characters: 251):
Because fear is a core emotional experience, emotional tools are needed to manage the
fright that runs haywire during a flashback. Healthy angering and crying can short-circuit fear
from morphing into the flashback-triggering cognitions of the critic.

chunk 3108 (Number of characters: 157):
THE FOUR PROCESSES OF GRIEVING
Grieving is at its most effective when the survivor can grieve in four ways: angering, crying,
verbal ventilating and feeling.

chunk 3110 (Number of characters: 415):
Angering: Diminishes Fear and Shame
Angering is the grieving technique of aggressively complaining about current or past losses and
injustices. Survivors need to anger - sometimes rage - about the intimidation, humiliation and
neglect that was passed off to them as nurturance in their childhoods. As they become adept at
grieving, they anger out their healthy resentment at their family’s pervasive lack of safety.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3111 (Number of characters: 93):
They
become incensed about the ten thousand betrayals of never being helped in times of need.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3112 (Number of characters: 79):
They
feel rage that there was never anyone to go to for guidance or protection.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3113 (Number of characters: 124):
They bellow that there
was no one to appeal to for fairness or appreciative recognition of their developmental
achievements.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3114 (Number of characters: 208):
My first book, The Tao of Fully Feeling, Harvesting Forgiveness Out Of Blame, explicates
in great detail a safe process for angering out childhood pain in a way that does not hurt the
survivor or anyone else.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3115 (Number of characters: 89):
In most cases, survivors do not have to directly anger at and blame their
living parents.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3116 (Number of characters: 86):
The key place to direct it is at your internalized parents - the parents of your past.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3117 (Number of characters: 72):
The most common exception to this occurs when a parent is still abusive.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3118 (Number of characters: 65):
This and other
exceptions are explored in depth in my first book.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3119 (Number of characters: 175):
Angering is therapeutic when the survivor rails against childhood trauma, and especially
when he rails against its living continuance in the self-hate processes of the critic.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3120 (Number of characters: 100):
Angrily saying
“No!” or “Shut Up!” to the critic, the deputy of his parents, externalizes his anger.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3121 (Number of characters: 140):
It stops him
from turning this anger against himself, and allows him to revive the lost instinct of defending
himself against unjust attack.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3122 (Number of characters: 61):
Additionally, angering rescues the survivor from toxic shame.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3123 (Number of characters: 81):
It rescues him from blindly

letting his parents’ venomous blame turn into shame.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3124 (Number of characters: 50):
Angering redirects blame back to where it
belongs.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3125 (Number of characters: 101):
It also augments his motivation to keep fighting to establish internal boundaries against
the critic.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3126 (Number of characters: 210):
Angering can be done alone or in the presence of a validating witness, such as a trusted
friend or therapist. Over time the vast majority of angering needs to be done silently in the
privacy of your own psyche.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3127 (Number of characters: 93):
This is the anger-empowered thought-stopping of shielding yourself
from inner critic attacks.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3128 (Number of characters: 225):
Many survivors are so identified with the critic that it becomes their whole identity. Such
survivors typically need to focus on fighting off the critic until they have established the healthy
ego function of self-protection.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3129 (Number of characters: 111):
Angering also serves to rescue a person from the childlike sense of powerlessness that she
is flashing back to.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3130 (Number of characters: 85):
It reminds her that she inhabits an adult body with which she can now defend
herself.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3131 (Number of characters: 72):
Through all these functions, angering serves to reduce or antidote fear.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3132 (Number of characters: 60):
It reawakens and
nurtures the instinct of self-preservation.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3133 (Number of characters: 80):
With practice it increasingly builds a sense of both
outer and inner boundaries.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3134 (Number of characters: 56):
These boundaries increasingly move us out of harm’s way.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3135 (Number of characters: 113):
They
offer safety from the bullying of others, and safety from the most damaging bully of all – the
inner critic.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3136 (Number of characters: 388):
Finally, angering can also empower the myriad thought corrections and substitutions needed
to establish the survivor’s belief in her own essential goodness and in the lovability of
discriminately chosen others. Angering bolsters her for the long-term, gradual process of
wrestling her self-image away from the critic and reeducating the psyche to make it both user-
and intimacy-friendly.

chunk 3157 (Number of characters: 121):
2. Crying: The Penultimate Soothing
In grieving, crying is the yin complementary process to the yang process of angering.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3158 (Number of characters: 59):
When we
are hurt, we instinctively feel sad as well as mad.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3159 (Number of characters: 92):
The newborn child, hurt by the loss of the
perfect security of the womb, howls an angry cry.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3160 (Number of characters: 88):
Crying is also an irreplaceable tool for cutting off the critic’s emotional fuel supply.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3161 (Number of characters: 83):
Tears
can release fear before it devolves into frightened and frightening thinking.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3162 (Number of characters: 76):
In fact, crying is
sometimes the only process that will resolve a flashback.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3163 (Number of characters: 92):
I have witnessed my own critic wither
into innocuousness hundreds of times after a good cry.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3164 (Number of characters: 136):
On thousands of other occasions, I have
seen my clients dissolve their fear, shame and self-abandonment with the solvent of their tears.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3165 (Number of characters: 141):
I
have also seen them then surface into a healthily angry place, determined to confront a current
unfairness that they now find unacceptable.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3166 (Number of characters: 122):
A client who left a year-long therapy to move elsewhere recently wrote to me about an
experience that she had with crying.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3167 (Number of characters: 85):

--------------------------------------------------

--------------------------------------------------

crying didn’t get through.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3180 (Number of characters: 128):
An additional benefit of crying is that unabashed tears stimulate the relaxation response of the
parasympathetic nervous system.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3181 (Number of characters: 104):
This counterbalances the excessive sympathetic nervous
system hyperarousal we experience in a flashback.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3182 (Number of characters: 231):
As we learn to grieve effectively, we allow ourselves to mourn about the lack of positive
parental attention in our childhoods. We feel sorrow about the horrible reality that parental
attention was typically negative and dangerous.

--------------------------------------------------

child who not appreciated and reflected as special, worthy, and easy to love.

chunk 3184 (Number of characters: 429):
Crying And Self-Compassion
When we greet our own tears with self-acceptance, crying awakens our develop- mentally
arrested instinct of self-compassion. Once we establish self-compassion through consistent and
repeated practice, it becomes the cornerstone of an increasing sense of self-esteem. When an
attitude of self-compassion becomes habitual, it can instantly antidote the self-abandonment that
so characterizes a flashback.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3185 (Number of characters: 122):
Moreover self-compassion creates a foundation from which we can build authentic,
intimacy-enhancing compassion for others.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3186 (Number of characters: 147):
The depth of our ability to be there for an intimate
generally depends on the depth of our capacity to practice unwavering allegiance to ourselves.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3187 (Number of characters: 133):
Crying And Angering In Concert
Crying and angering are the two key emotional tools for releasing the pain of the abandonment
mélange.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3188 (Number of characters: 56):
Typically we need both processes to attain full release.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3189 (Number of characters: 50):
Crying and angering together differs from whining.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3190 (Number of characters: 355):
Whining is a type of emoting that gives
grieving a bad name. Whining is a delicate subject because many people who complain and/or
cry in a healthy manner are pathologized as “whiners.” Dysfunctional whining however is often
an unhealthy mixture of angering and crying, where either the anger or sadness is repressed but
leaks through in a grating manner.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3191 (Number of characters: 30):
Here are two examples of this.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3192 (Number of characters: 194):
When a hurt person only knows how to express anger, his
repressed sadness unconsciously seeps into his anger in a way that makes him sound like a
martyr or someone with delusions of persecution.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3193 (Number of characters: 177):
Because there is no substantial release of his
sadness, no amount of whining brings him relief, and he can angrily whine endlessly in a way
that exhausts his listener’s empathy.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3194 (Number of characters: 148):
Similarly when a hurt person is only able to cry, repressed
anger tinges her sadness and makes it sound like irritable and interminable bellyaching.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3195 (Number of characters: 70):
One of
my clients calls this “anger coming through a very small hole.”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3196 (Number of characters: 270):
Many of us suffer from a socialization process that relegates angering to men and crying to
women. From an early age boys are shamed for crying while girls are treated similarly for
angering. Hence boys become men who can only vent their emotional pain through angering.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3197 (Number of characters: 86):
Sadness then must morph into an irritable mood, aggravated whining or angry catharsis.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3198 (Number of characters: 160):
Similarly, some women unconsciously try to squeeze their anger out by whining or crying in a
way that devolves into feeling stuck in helplessness and self-pity.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3199 (Number of characters: 71):
Many men also rely on anger to carry all of their emotional expression.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3200 (Number of characters: 59):
They get mad when
they are scared, humiliated or feel down.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3201 (Number of characters: 70):
Many women, on the other hand, can only try to cry out
these feelings.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3202 (Number of characters: 52):
This typically results in a very incomplete release.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3203 (Number of characters: 39):
The full motion of emotion is
arrested.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3204 (Number of characters: 76):
Half of the normal emotional release process is then stymied in each gender.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3205 (Number of characters: 152):
Without complete emoting of his hurt, a survivor can become stuck in moodiness. His
unexpressed emotion deteriorates into a stagnant and lingering mood.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3206 (Number of characters: 121):
This moodiness can range

from festering resentment to a shame-tainted self-pity that is anything but self-compassionate.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3207 (Number of characters: 122):
Blocked anger can degenerate into bitter sullenness, and blocked sadness can deteriorate into
melancholic self-indulgence.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3208 (Number of characters: 141):
How this contrasts with the healthy grieving of my six-year old son, who has to periodically
grieve the loss of his narcissistic entitlement.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3209 (Number of characters: 74):
Less and less does he get to be what Freud called:
“His majesty the baby”.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3210 (Number of characters: 115):
As his previously apt entitlement lessens, he mildly bawls at new rules that are age-
appropriate for him to learn.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3211 (Number of characters: 92):
Bawling is the evolution of the baby’s angry cry, and it releases
pain through angry crying.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3212 (Number of characters: 127):
He howled a bit this afternoon about the “unfairness” of having to do
his homework after school before he could play some more.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3213 (Number of characters: 128):
As we climbed the 37 stairs to our home, he punctuated his crying with a flurry of angry
condemnations: “I don’t like you daddy.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3214 (Number of characters: 16):
You’re not fair.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3215 (Number of characters: 33):
I’m not going to be your friend”.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3216 (Number of characters: 182):
I am
grateful for this healthy grieving, and still somewhat amazed that I can so calmly “hold” it as I
help him metabolize this latest loss of early childhood narcissistic privilege.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3217 (Number of characters: 118):
By the time we get to the top of the stairs he opens the door and genuinely peals out a
guileless laugh: “Daddy, look!

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3218 (Number of characters: 27):
Picachu fell off the table.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3219 (Number of characters: 213):
Daddy can we play Pokémon after I
practice writing my letters?”
Grieving has almost instantly delivered him from painful loss into eager apprehension of
what is fun about life and what there is to look forward to.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3220 (Number of characters: 150):
How frequently, I see my clients
reemerge into remembering their real, present-day resourcefulness once they have grieved
through a painful flashback.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3221 (Number of characters: 158):
When we can both anger and cry while re-experiencing our early abandonment in a
flashback, we can obtain a more complete release from the abandonment mélange.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3222 (Number of characters: 125):
Each survivor
does well to assess whether his angering or crying is blocked or stultified, and to then work at
recovering it.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3223 (Number of characters: 111):

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3224 (Number of characters: 80):

crying are blocked.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3225 (Number of characters: 134):

and tears from the stultifying prohibitions of the critic.

chunk 3226 (Number of characters: 83):
If you are unable cry or feel angry, focusing on your breath may help you to emote.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3227 (Number of characters: 128):
This is
especially true if you attend to the sensations in your abdominal region as it expands and
contracts during respiration.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3228 (Number of characters: 154):
Deep, slow and rhythmic breathing stretches and expands various
visceral muscles and internal organs in ways that sometimes bring feelings into awareness.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3229 (Number of characters: 130):
If this does not help, Holotropic Rebirthing and Reichian therapy employ special breathing
techniques to help free stuck emotions.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3230 (Number of characters: 91):
I know many survivors who first reclaimed their capacity
to emote through these techniques.

chunk 3231 (Number of characters: 74):
3. Verbal Ventilation: The Golden Path To Intimacy
“Joy shared is doubled.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3232 (Number of characters: 25):
Sorrow shared is halved.”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3233 (Number of characters: 74):
- Old Chinese saying

Verbal ventilation is the third process of grieving.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3234 (Number of characters: 55):
It is the penultimate resolver of
emotional flashbacks.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3235 (Number of characters: 98):
Verbal ventilation is speaking or writing in a manner that airs out and
releases painful feelings.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3236 (Number of characters: 142):
When we let our words spring from what we feel, language is imbued
with emotion, and pain can be released through what we say, think or write.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3237 (Number of characters: 231):
As our grieving
proficiency increases, we can verbally ventilate about our losses, using shame-dissolving
language to tell the story of how we were unfairly deprived of our birthright to be welcomed into
a family that cherished us.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3238 (Number of characters: 179):
My favorite technique to enhance verbal ventilation is to encourage the survivor to talk in
an uncensored manner about whatever comes to his mind while he focuses on his feelings.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3239 (Number of characters: 112):
If his
feelings are not in awareness, focusing on bodily sensations can provide rich grist for verbally
venting.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3240 (Number of characters: 260):
Verbal Ventilation is therapeutic to the degree that a person’s words are colored by and
descriptive of the anger, sadness, fear, shame and/or depression she feels. Ventilation that is
liberally punctuated with actual crying or angering is especially powerful.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3241 (Number of characters: 134):
Theoretical Neuroscience Of Verbal Ventilation
Verbal ventilation is a tool that can remediate brain changes that are caused by Cptsd.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3242 (Number of characters: 329):
Susan
Vaughan’s MRI research demonstrates that emotional flashbacks over-activate the emotionally
oriented right-brain and under-activate the thinking-oriented left-brain. With this hemispheric
polarization, there is an overwhelming reemergence of childhood pain that is emotionally
remembered by the memory-oriented right-brain.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3243 (Number of characters: 304):
At the same time, the survivor loses access to
the higher cognitive functions of her left-brain. This temporary loss of cognitive perspective
explains why it is so hard for a survivor to realize that she is only flashing back, and not really
lost in the danger, helplessness and hopelessness of the past.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3244 (Number of characters: 144):
Verbal ventilation, at its most potent, is the therapeutic process of bringing left-brain
cognition to intense right-brain emotional activation.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3245 (Number of characters: 149):
It fosters the recoveree’s ability to put
words to feelings, and ultimately to accurately interpret and communicate about his various
feeling states.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3246 (Number of characters: 187):
When this process is repeated sufficiently, new neural pathways grow that allow
the left- and right-brain to work together so that the person can actually think and feel at the
same time.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3247 (Number of characters: 90):
The corpus callosum is the part of the brain that connects the left and right hemispheres.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3248 (Number of characters: 75):
Research shows that the corpus callosum is smaller in some Cptsd survivors.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3249 (Number of characters: 117):
Further research
shows that the brain can generate new neurons and new neuronal connections to remedy this
situation.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3250 (Number of characters: 135):
Thinking And Feeling Simultaneously
When a survivor becomes proficient at verbal ventilation, she heals a crucial developmental
arrest.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3251 (Number of characters: 118):
She learns to think about feeling states in a way that creates healthy, helpful and
appropriate responses to feelings.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3252 (Number of characters: 89):
These responses are characteristically respectful to herself and
to whomever she is with.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3253 (Number of characters: 111):
In his book, Emotional Intelligence, Daniel Goleman writes that this is
a core trait of emotional intelligence.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3254 (Number of characters: 238):
With continued practice, verbal ventilation coordinates the left and right hemispheres of the

brain so that whenever the right-brain is hyper-activated in flashback, the left-brain is also fully
engaged [this also can be seen in an MRI].

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3255 (Number of characters: 138):
With the left-brain back on line, the survivor can
remember to use the flashback management steps to successfully help manage a flashback.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3256 (Number of characters: 113):
As with angering and crying, verbal ventilation is only effective when it is liberated from
the critic’s control.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3257 (Number of characters: 86):
In early recovery, verbal ventilation can easily shift into verbal self-
flagellation.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3258 (Number of characters: 116):
Verbally ventilating from the self-attacking or drasticizing perspective of the critic is
rarely effective grieving.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3259 (Number of characters: 129):
Instead it typically triggers or intensifies flashbacks, which in turn
frequently generates self- and intimacy-injuring behavior.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3260 (Number of characters: 134):
Many survivors in early recovery are unable to notice their unconscious shifts into over-
identifying with the critic’s point of view.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3261 (Number of characters: 149):
When this is the case, they usually need the help of a
therapist or sufficiently recovered intimate to help them recognize and neutralize the critic.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3262 (Number of characters: 68):
Verbal Ventilation is an especially transformative grieving process.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3263 (Number of characters: 196):
It not only promotes the
same type of fear-release and shame-dissolution as angering and crying, but also helps to expose
the manifold guises of the critic’s self-attack and fear-arousal programs.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3264 (Number of characters: 136):
Verbal ventilation also
helps us to identify and communicate about needs that have long been unmet because of our
childhood abandonment.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3265 (Number of characters: 118):
Verbally Ventilating Alone
Verbally venting alone, where no one else can hear you, is a helpful therapeutic technique.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3266 (Number of characters: 83):
Untraumatized children do it frequently in their play, much to their great benefit.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3267 (Number of characters: 73):
If your room is
not private, you can drive your car to somewhere that is.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3268 (Number of characters: 88):
Typically it takes considerable silencing of the critic to regain this therapeutic tool.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3269 (Number of characters: 127):
This
tool is so powerful and restorative that it is worth many skirmishes with the critic to reclaim it for
your Cptsd toolbox.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3270 (Number of characters: 102):
One of my clients kept an 18” length of rubber hose and a phone book on the passenger seat
of her car.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3271 (Number of characters: 180):
During the year that she was doing the lion’s share of her anger work, she would hit
the hose loudly on the phonebook whenever her brainwashing, abusive father came up in her
mind.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3272 (Number of characters: 141):
She would of course pull over for this, and found over time that she could do it
inconspicuously in places with surprisingly limited privacy.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3273 (Number of characters: 71):
It was remarkable to me how much
critic-shrinking she did in that year.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3274 (Number of characters: 174):
Dissociation Deadens Verbal Ventilation
It is important to differentiate verbal ventilation from dissociative flights of fantasy and
obsessive bouts of unproductive worrying.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3275 (Number of characters: 225):
Dissociation is a defense that children develop to
distract and protect themselves from the overwhelming pain of their abandonment. As
unsupported children, we have to dissociate because we are not able to effectively grieve.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3276 (Number of characters: 87):
We
have to protect ourselves by not allowing the full brunt of our pain into awareness.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3277 (Number of characters: 232):
As we saw in chapter 6, there are two common types of dissociation: right-brain
dissociation and left-brain dissociation. Right-brain dissociation can be seen as classical dissociation and as the defense most
common to freeze types.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3278 (Number of characters: 102):
It is the right-brain process of numbing out against intense feeling or
incessant inner critic attack.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3279 (Number of characters: 52):
Dissociation is once again a process of distraction.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3280 (Number of characters: 96):
Survivors

commonly experience it as getting lost in fantasy, fogginess, TV, tiredness or sleep.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3281 (Number of characters: 98):
Verbally reporting from a place of self-distraction is in fact the opposite of verbal
ventilation.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3282 (Number of characters: 144):
Getting lost in daydream-like descriptions of improbable salvation fantasies is a
common way that clients avoid their pain in a therapy session.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3283 (Number of characters: 133):
Another common example is
recounting long elaborate dreams that are devoid of emotional content or serious attempts at
introspection.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3284 (Number of characters: 148):
Early in my career, I worked briefly with a freeze-fight type client who was stuck in an
avoidance process of endlessly monologing about her dreams.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3285 (Number of characters: 52):
Dina recalled them in lifeless,
excruciating detail.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3286 (Number of characters: 92):
In fact, her deadened delivery was emblematic of the way she talked about
almost everything.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3287 (Number of characters: 130):
Dina was an intern-therapist who locked up her vulnerability with a belief that dream work
was the piece de resistance of therapy.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3288 (Number of characters: 131):
My attempts to guide her into exploring her deeper
underlying experience were routinely met with the hackles of her fight response.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3289 (Number of characters: 192):
I was
unfortunately not experienced enough at the time to help her see that this was a dissociative
defense that she had constructed as a child to protect herself from a very intrusive mother.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3290 (Number of characters: 167):
Sadly,
she left therapy disgruntled and still trapped in the isolation that was born of alienating others
with her distant, abstract and non-interactive dissertations.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3291 (Number of characters: 65):
Left-Brain Dissociation
Left-brain dissociation is obsessiveness.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3292 (Number of characters: 164):
Commonly, this ranges in severity from dwelling on a
singular worry… to repetitively cycling through a list of worries… to panicky drasticizing and
catastrophizing.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3293 (Number of characters: 180):
This type of dissociation from internal pain strands the survivor in unhelpful
ruminations about issues that are unrelated or minimally related to the true nature of her
suffering.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3294 (Number of characters: 27):
This is an example of this.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3295 (Number of characters: 118):
Your friend complains incessantly about the bad weather or the
unfairness of people who do not use their turn signals.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3296 (Number of characters: 143):
He cannot stop grumbling because no
amount of complaining about the wrong thing releases the real pain that is driving his obsessive
grievance.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3297 (Number of characters: 219):
If he were able to look deeper at what is really troubling him, he might learn that his
wife’s constructive feedback is continuously flashing him back into the fear and shame he felt
when his mother verbally abused him.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3298 (Number of characters: 64):
Left-brain dissociation can also be a process of trivialization.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3299 (Number of characters: 128):
This occurs when the survivor
over-focuses on superficial external concerns to distract himself from upsetting inner experience.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3300 (Number of characters: 117):
Becoming overly preoccupied with sports statistics or the lives of Hollywood celebrities are
common examples of this.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3301 (Number of characters: 108):
This is not, of course, to say that such interests are not worthwhile
when they are pursued with moderation.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3302 (Number of characters: 73):
Finally, left-brain dissociation can also be seen in intellectualization.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3303 (Number of characters: 93):
This is what the
novelist, Ian McEwan, called the “high-walled fortress of focused thinking”.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3304 (Number of characters: 135):
Some survivors
over-rely on reasoning and lofty dialogue to protect themselves from the potentially messy and
painful world of feeling.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3305 (Number of characters: 121):
Even the highest levels of creative thinking can deteriorate into an
obsessive defense when they are excessively engaged.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3306 (Number of characters: 153):
Verbal Ventilation Heals Abandonment

When we share what is emotionally important to us, we learn to connect with others in a
meaningful and healing way.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3307 (Number of characters: 105):
This applies to sharing concerns that excite and please us, as well
as those that frighten or depress us.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3308 (Number of characters: 137):
Perhaps there was no more detrimental consequence of our
childhood abandonment than being forced to habitually hide our authentic selves.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3309 (Number of characters: 123):
Many of us
come out of childhood believing that what we have to say is as uninteresting to others as it was
to our parents.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3310 (Number of characters: 51):
We must repudiate this damaging legacy of the past.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3311 (Number of characters: 59):
Verbal ventilation is the key way that
people make friends.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3312 (Number of characters: 144):
It parallels the way tender touch, soothing voice, and welcoming facial
expressions helps infants and toddlers establish bonding and attachment.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3313 (Number of characters: 164):
When we practice the
emotionally based communication of verbal ventilation in a safe environment, we repair the
damage of not having had this need met in childhood.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3314 (Number of characters: 145):
This in turn opens up the possibility of
finally attaining the verbal-emotional intimacy that is an essential lifelong need for all human
beings.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3315 (Number of characters: 80):
Committing to this type of practice typically requires courage and perseverance.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3316 (Number of characters: 128):
Authentic
sharing can be triggering, and sometimes flashes the survivor back to being punished or rejected
for being vulnerable.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3317 (Number of characters: 127):
Therapy, individual or group, can help greatly to overcome and work
through these obstacles to vitalizing your self-expression.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3318 (Number of characters: 112):
Verbal Ventilation And Intimacy
Reciprocal verbal ventilation is the highway to intimacy in adult relationships.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3319 (Number of characters: 113):
Sufficient practice
with a safe enough other brings genuine experiences of comforting and restorative connection.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3320 (Number of characters: 117):
For me and many of my clients, such experiences are more alleviating of loneliness than we had
ever thought possible.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3321 (Number of characters: 184):
Nowhere is this truer than with mutual commiseration. Mutual commiseration is the process
in which two intimates are reciprocally sympathetic to each other’s troubles and difficulties.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3322 (Number of characters: 74):
It is
the deepest most intimate channel to intimacy – profounder than sex.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3323 (Number of characters: 128):
Mutual commiseration also
typically promotes a spontaneous opening into many levels of light-hearted and spontaneous
connecting.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3324 (Number of characters: 166):
As deep and meaningful connection with another becomes more available and frequent, the
survivor increasingly experiences the shrinking of his abandonment depression.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3325 (Number of characters: 110):
I must reiterate here that some popular bodywork approaches to trauma belittle the
usefulness of talk therapy.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3326 (Number of characters: 125):
While somatic work is a vital tool of recovery, it alone cannot shrink
your toxic critic or resuscitate your self-expression.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3327 (Number of characters: 107):
Cognitive work, especially that which is
empowered by verbal ventilation, is fundamental to Cptsd recovery.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3328 (Number of characters: 112):
With that said, we will move on to Feeling, the fourth grieving process – a process that is a
type of body work.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3329 (Number of characters: 154):
Feeling is a way of focusing on somatic experience that enables us to reclaim
our ability to experience full, relaxed and vital inhabitancy of our bodies.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3330 (Number of characters: 2):
4.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3331 (Number of characters: 300):
Feeling: Passively Working Through Grief

“Feeling is the antithesis of pain…the more pain one feels, the less pain one suffers”
– Arthur Janov
Ongoing engagement in the active grieving processes of angering, crying and verbal ventilation
can help us to discover the fourth grieving process: Feeling.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3332 (Number of characters: 228):
Feeling is a subtler, passive process than emoting. It is best illustrated by contrasting the
concepts of emoting and feeling. Emoting is when we cry, anger out, or verbally ventilate the
energy of an inner emotional experience.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3333 (Number of characters: 264):
Feeling, on the other hand, is the inactive process of
staying present to internal emotional experience without reacting. In recovery then, feeling is
surrendering to our internal experiences of pain without judging or resisting them, and without
emoting them out.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3334 (Number of characters: 60):
Feeling is a kinesthetic rather than a cognitive experience.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3335 (Number of characters: 129):
It is the process of shifting the
focus of your awareness off of thinking and onto your affects, energetic states and sensations.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3336 (Number of characters: 77):
It
is the proverbial “getting out of your head” and “getting into your body.”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3337 (Number of characters: 387):
As a grieving process, feeling involves consciously reversing the learned survival
mechanism of clamping down on pain to banish it from awareness. Feeling “occurs” when we direct our attention to an emotionally or physically painful state,
and surrender to this experience without resistance. When we relax acceptingly into our pain, we
can learn to gently absorb it into our experience.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3338 (Number of characters: 136):
Feeling then functions as if our awareness is a
solvent that dissolves and metabolizes the affect, energy and sensation of our emotions.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3339 (Number of characters: 182):
Feeling Can Heal Digestive Problems
The practice of feeling is also similar to healthy food digestion whereby a relaxed digestive tract
allows us to effectively assimilate nutrients.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3340 (Number of characters: 128):
If, however, we suppress or repress our feelings, our
bodies typically armor and tighten, especially along the alimentary canal.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3341 (Number of characters: 116):
I believe digestive tract-tightening can create the types of digestive problems that frequently
co-occur with Cptsd.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3342 (Number of characters: 222):
Diarrhea, for instance, is sometimes an emotionally-caused, physical
response to intense danger. When this occurs, fear triggers the sympathetic nervous system into
further triggering an immediate evacuation of the bowels.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3343 (Number of characters: 29):
Nausea can operate similarly.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3344 (Number of characters: 152):
Constipation, on the other hand, is sometimes a tightening in the alimentary canal that hampers
the peristaltic motion necessary for healthy evacuation.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3345 (Number of characters: 372):
The Emotional-Physical Connection
There is often a close relationship between emotion and physical sensation. Physical sensations
in the body often co-occur with feelings. Moreover, sensations of tightness and tension can
develop as a defense against feelings. As unexpressed feelings accumulate, a greater degree of
muscular tension is necessary to keep them under wraps.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3346 (Number of characters: 109):
A child who is repeatedly punished for emoting learns to be afraid of inner emotional
experience and tightens

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3347 (Number of characters: 164):
[armors] the musculature of her body in an effort to hold feelings in and
to banish them from awareness. Holding your breath is a further manifestation of armoring.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3348 (Number of characters: 134):
It is an especially common way
of keeping feelings at bay, as breathing naturally brings your awareness down to the level of

feeling.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3349 (Number of characters: 191):
As my client, Kylie, free associated on the feelings of a particularly intense flashback, she
painfully recalled the traumas she underwent trying to avoid her mother’s punishments for
crying.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3350 (Number of characters: 158):
Her worst memory was of the day in the supermarket, when her mother’s scolding rained
down upon her as she tried to suppress her rapidly mounting urge to cry.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3351 (Number of characters: 98):
Knowing the worst
punishment would ensue if she cried, she held her breath to hold back the tears.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3352 (Number of characters: 96):
She held it so
long that she fainted from lack of oxygen and cracked her head open on the floor.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3353 (Number of characters: 103):
Her mother,
mortified by what she saw as public humiliation, beat her black and blue when she got home.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3354 (Number of characters: 87):
It took us two years of trust-building work to bring this incident back into awareness.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3355 (Number of characters: 154):
She
then cried her first tears since when it happened, three decades earlier. These were also the first
tears she could ever remember that brought relief.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3356 (Number of characters: 145):
The technique of focusing your awareness on physical sensations in your body can help you
to become more proficient at the practice of “feeling”.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3357 (Number of characters: 164):
With enough practice, paying attention to
tightness in your face, throat, heart or belly area brings feelings into awareness, where they can
simply be felt through.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3358 (Number of characters: 154):
However, in your early experiences of focusing on sensations, feelings
may come up so strongly that you will benefit from allowing yourself to emote them.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3359 (Number of characters: 203):
Balancing Feeling And Emoting
When we become more mindful of the subtle sensations of feelings, the passive grieving process
of feeling through them complements the active processes of grieving them out.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3360 (Number of characters: 129):
We are typically
in advanced recovery when we can both emote out and feel through our anger, sadness, fear,
shame and depression.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3361 (Number of characters: 223):
Furthermore, feeling also helps us to bring emotions into awareness that need to be grieved
out through active, cathartic emoting. Thus, grieving is especially profound when we can fluidly
shift between feeling and emoting.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3362 (Number of characters: 80):
Sometimes we will only need to fully feel and accept the
sensations of our pain.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3363 (Number of characters: 147):
Other times we will want to verbally ventilate about our pain with
someone who gives us full permission to color our words with angering and tears.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3364 (Number of characters: 176):
Learning To Feel
As a survivor becomes more adept at angering and crying, fear of his feelings will decrease, and
opportunities to learn to simply feel will present themselves.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3365 (Number of characters: 262):
He can engage these opportunities
by passively attuning to the more subtle sensations of his anger and sadness. Over time this practice will build his ability to stay passively present to the sensations of his
deeper feelings – to his fear, shame and depression.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3366 (Number of characters: 145):
But in early stages, this awareness will often
morph into the need to actively emote them out – to grieve himself out of the abandonment
mélange.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3367 (Number of characters: 132):
Eventually, however, his abandonment mélange feelings will also be digested and
worked through purely with the solvent of awareness.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3368 (Number of characters: 154):
This also applies to anxiety which is often
fear just below the level of awareness. With sufficient practice, anxiety can often be felt through
passively.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3369 (Number of characters: 66):
We will explore this process in greater depth in the next chapter.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3370 (Number of characters: 109):
An Exercise In Feeling

Here is an exercise to help you enhance your ability to feel and grieve through pain.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3371 (Number of characters: 100):
Visualize
yourself as time-traveling back to a place in the past when you felt especially abandoned.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3372 (Number of characters: 407):
See
your adult self taking your abandoned child onto your lap and comforting her in various painful
emotional states or situations. You can comfort her/him verbally: “I feel such sorrow that you
were so abandoned and that you felt so alone so much of the time. I love you even more when
you are stuck in this abandonment pain – especially because you had to endure it for so long with
no one to comfort you.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3373 (Number of characters: 36):
That shouldn’t have happened to you.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3374 (Number of characters: 33):
It shouldn’t happen to any child.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3375 (Number of characters: 28):
Let me comfort and hold you.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3376 (Number of characters: 38):
You don’t have to rush to get over it.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3377 (Number of characters: 66):
It is not your fault. You
didn’t cause it and you’re not to blame.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3378 (Number of characters: 30):
You don’t have to do anything.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3379 (Number of characters: 21):
Just let me hold you.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3380 (Number of characters: 15):
Take your time.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3381 (Number of characters: 53):
I love you always and care about you no matter what.”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3382 (Number of characters: 129):
I highly recommend practicing this even if it feels inauthentic, and even if it requires a great
deal of fending off your critic.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3383 (Number of characters: 129):
Keep practicing and eventually, you will have a genuine
experience of feeling self-compassion for the traumatized child you were.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3384 (Number of characters: 81):
When that occurs, you
will know that your recovery work had reached a deep level.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3385 (Number of characters: 240):
Techniques To Invite And Enhance Grieving
Tears were hard to come by after my first breakthrough cry in early adulthood. I would often
long for the incredible relief I felt on that occasion, but unlike angering, I could not force my
crying.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3386 (Number of characters: 57):
I have come to learn that this is typical of many people.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3387 (Number of characters: 118):
My friend Malcolm, suffering the
same frustration, wanted to cry so badly, that he squeezed lemon juice into his eyes.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3388 (Number of characters: 86):
He was able
to laugh about it later, but solemnly warned me to never try such a thing.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3389 (Number of characters: 105):
This is a list that evolved out of our ongoing discussions about how to coax out the tears of
a good cry.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3390 (Number of characters: 126):
As with all the advice in this book, please feel free to use only the ones that seem to
fit, and in whatever order seems best.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3391 (Number of characters: 89):
Moreover, different combinations may enhance either
crying or angering, or both together.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3392 (Number of characters: 128):
Finally, sometimes nothing works, but I encourage you to
try techniques that intuitively seem like they may work more than once.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3393 (Number of characters: 62):
1. Find a safe and comfortable place where you won’t be heard.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3394 (Number of characters: 83):
2. Close your eyes and remember a time when you felt compassionate towards someone.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3395 (Number of characters: 107):
This can be from real life, or from reading a book or a poem, or from watching a movie
or moving news item.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3396 (Number of characters: 123):
3. Invoke self-compassion via the memory of someone who was kind to you, or imagine
someone you think would be kind to you.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3397 (Number of characters: 23):
I would be kind to you.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3398 (Number of characters: 110):
4. Verbally ventilate about what is bothering you in a journal or aloud to a real or
imagined friend or to me.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3399 (Number of characters: 2):
5.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3400 (Number of characters: 51):
Imagine yourself being comforted by a Higher Power.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3401 (Number of characters: 79):
See yourself in the lap of a kind
higher power or actual person who seems kind.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3402 (Number of characters: 53):
[Santa Claus once worked well for one
of my clients].

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3403 (Number of characters: 211):
6. Remember a time when you felt better from crying or angering, or seeing someone else
cry in real life or in a movie. 7. Remember a time when being angry, or when someone else being angry, saved you
from harm.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3404 (Number of characters: 2):
8.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3405 (Number of characters: 64):
Imagine your anger forming a protective fiery shield around you.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3406 (Number of characters: 2):
9.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3407 (Number of characters: 85):
Imagine your tears or anger carrying any fear, shame or depression up and out of you.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3408 (Number of characters: 3):
10.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3409 (Number of characters: 49):
Imagine holding your inner child compassionately.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3410 (Number of characters: 85):
Tell the child it’s normal and okay
to feel sad or mad about feeling bad or hurt.
11.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3411 (Number of characters: 64):
