chunk 3418 (Number of characters: 229):
An elaborating note on technique #15: Several clients told me that the scene in the 1976 movie
Network where the main character yells out a window: “We’re not taking it anymore”, helped
them to bring up and ventilate their anger.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3419 (Number of characters: 255):
Finally, if reading this book does not begin to release the critic’s stranglehold on your ability to
grieve, please consider working with a therapist or support group to help you work through the
shame that the critic uses to spoil your ability to grieve.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3420 (Number of characters: 488):
THE MAP: MANAGING THE ABANDONMENT DEPRESSION
Cptsd burdens us with a hair-trigger susceptibility to painful emotional flashbacks. Flashbacks,
as we have seen, are layers of defensive reactions to the abandonment depression. These
reactions include physical, behavioral, cognitive, emotional and relational responses to the
reemerging sense of danger and despair that plagued our childhood abandonment. This chapter presents a map of these layered, defensive reactions to abandonment pain.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3421 (Number of characters: 83):
This
map shows you which reactions are most important to work on at any given time.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3422 (Number of characters: 83):
It includes a
strategy for reducing harmful reactions that are no longer necessary.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3423 (Number of characters: 219):
This strategy involves self-
compassionately soothing yourself when you are trapped in fear and/or depression, so that you
do not launch into intensified fear, toxic shame, critic attacks, or self-damaging 4F reactions.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3424 (Number of characters: 119):
Cycles Of Reactivity
This section presents a verbal diagram of the layering of our reactions in an emotional flashback.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3425 (Number of characters: 218):
Experiences of depression and abandonment trigger us into fear and shame, which then activates
panicky inner critic thinking, which in turn launches us into an adrenalized fight, flight, freeze or
fawn trauma response.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3426 (Number of characters: 55):
This is how that reactivity takes place in a flashback.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3427 (Number of characters: 38):
A survivor wakes up feeling
depressed.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3428 (Number of characters: 143):
Because childhood experience has conditioned her to believe that she is unworthy and
unacceptable in this state, she feels anxious and ashamed.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3429 (Number of characters: 108):
This in turn activates her inner critic to
scare her with perfectionistic rants: “No wonder no one likes me.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3430 (Number of characters: 199):
I’ve got to get my lazy,
worthless ass out of bed or I’ll end up like that homeless, bag lady in the park!”
Retraumatized by her own inner voice, she then launches into her most habitual 4F
behavior.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3431 (Number of characters: 306):
She either lashes out domineeringly at the nearest person [Fight] – or she launches
busily into anxious productivity [Flight] – or she flips on the TV and foggily tunes out or dozes
off again [Freeze] – or she self-abandoningly redirects her attention to figuring out how to fix a

friend’s problem [Fawn].

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3432 (Number of characters: 100):
All this typically happens so quickly that we do not notice the fear and shame, or the inner
critic.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3433 (Number of characters: 130):
The first thing that we usually begin to notice in early recovery is that suddenly we are
engaged in our most typical 4F response.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3434 (Number of characters: 53):
As recovery progresses we become aware of the critic.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3435 (Number of characters: 80):
Eventually this promotes mindfulness of the fear and shame that fuel the critic.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3436 (Number of characters: 84):
And finally in
later recovery, we become aware of the abandonment depression itself.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3437 (Number of characters: 61):
Unfortunately this dynamic also commonly operates in reverse.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3438 (Number of characters: 118):
This can occur in early recovery
when you suddenly notice that you have slipped back into a dysfunctional 4F behavior.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3439 (Number of characters: 97):
This then
triggers you into new self-attacking criticism, which then amps up your fear and shame.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3440 (Number of characters: 203):
This in
turn increases your abandonment depression as the process of self-abandonment runs riot. Desperate to escape the deathlike feelings of the abandonment depression, the initial cycle
revs up again.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3441 (Number of characters: 131):
Depression creates fear and shame which fuels venomous critic attacks and
launches you into fighting, fleeing, freezing or fawning.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3442 (Number of characters: 120):
Survivors can careen back and forth through these layered reactions creating perpetual
motion cycles of internal trauma.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3443 (Number of characters: 128):
I believe this process also accounts for the spiraling down feeling that many survivors
report having during intense flashbacks.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3444 (Number of characters: 175):
This is a diagram of these dynamics:
ABANDONMENT DEPRESSION 
 FEAR & SHAME 
 INNER
CRITIC 
 4Fs
Let us look at a case example of how triggering works in the reverse direction.

--------------------------------------------------

My mega-
perfectionistic, flight-type client, Mario, came into our session five minutes late.

time he had been late in two years of therapy.

chunk 3447 (Number of characters: 103):
Mario was in full-fledged flight, sweating from racing up the hill from his parking space a

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3448 (Number of characters: 52):
He told me he just could not sit on the couch today.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3449 (Number of characters: 48):
Could he please just pace in
front of the couch.

“I hate being late.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3451 (Number of characters: 82):

driving on the freeway!”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3453 (Number of characters: 78):

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3457 (Number of characters: 88):
This was the real helplessness and

--------------------------------------------------

The long exile into self-abandonment was temporarily

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3463 (Number of characters: 84):
We were then able to look at the cycle of reactivity that had turned into a cyclone.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3464 (Number of characters: 145):
Triggered by a traffic jam into the “terrible danger” of being late, Cptsd launched him into his
flight response as he raced to get to my office.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3465 (Number of characters: 139):

--------------------------------------------------

and shame.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3468 (Number of characters: 113):

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3469 (Number of characters: 290):
Had he not been able to do this, he most likely would have continued attacking himself with

session back to obsessively and compulsively racing through his day driven by his infinite to-do
list.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3470 (Number of characters: 155):
The Layers Of Dissociation In The Cycle Of Reactivity
All the layered reactions in the cycle of reactivity are defenses against the abandonment
depression.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3471 (Number of characters: 72):
Each layer is also a defense against all of the other layers beneath it.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3472 (Number of characters: 46):
As such, each
layer is a type of dissociation.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3473 (Number of characters: 143):
When we are triggered and lost in a 4F response, we fight, flee,
freeze or fawn to disassociate ourselves from the painful voice of the critic.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3474 (Number of characters: 96):
On a deeper layer,
the critic is also distracting and disassociating us from our emotional pain.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3475 (Number of characters: 109):
Moreover, fear and
shame dis-associate us from the bottom layer - the terrible abandonment depression itself.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3476 (Number of characters: 12):
Dissociation

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3477 (Number of characters: 134):
[the psychological term for disassociation], then, is the process of rendering all
these levels less conscious or totally unconscious.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3478 (Number of characters: 309):
As recovery progresses and we learn to stay present enough to the critic to begin shrinking
it, we become increasingly aware of the fear and shame that underlies it. With sufficient feeling
into fear and shame, we notice that fear and shame cover up the deadened feelings of the
abandonment depression itself.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3479 (Number of characters: 138):
Learning to stay self-supportingly present to the feelings of this
depression is the deepest level, bottom-of-the-barrel work of recovery.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3480 (Number of characters: 73):
When we are able to do
this, our recovering has reached a profound level.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3481 (Number of characters: 191):
Parental Abandonment Creates Self-Abandonment
Once again, we had to dissociate with these layered defenses because we were not
developmentally capable of managing the pain of being abandoned.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3482 (Number of characters: 142):
We could not tolerate feeling
the fear, shame and inconsolableness of it. We could not use our anger and tears to release this
shame and fear.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3483 (Number of characters: 103):
We could not bear to stay tuned in to the incessantly persecutory critic, over
which we were powerless.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3484 (Number of characters: 42):
We could only fight, flee, freeze or fawn.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3485 (Number of characters: 67):
Over time this elaborate
cycle of self-abandonment became habitual.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3486 (Number of characters: 49):
Chronic emotional abandonment devastates a child.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3487 (Number of characters: 63):
It naturally makes her feel and appear

deadened and depressed.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3488 (Number of characters: 76):
Functional parents respond to a child’s depression with concern and
comfort.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3489 (Number of characters: 387):
Abandoning parents respond to the child with anger, disgust and/or further
abandonment, which in turn exacerbate the fear, shame and despair that become the
abandonment mélange. Deconstructing Self-Abandonment
Because of our parents’ rejection, the mildest hint of depression, no matter how functional or
appropriate, can instantly flash us back into our original abandonment depression.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3490 (Number of characters: 110):
The capacity
to self-nurturingly weather any experience of depression, no matter how mild, remains
unrealized.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3491 (Number of characters: 92):
Our original experience of parental abandonment has morphed into habitual self-
abandonment.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3492 (Number of characters: 150):
We can gradually deconstruct the self-abandoning habit of reacting to depression with fear
and shame, inner critic catastrophizing, and 4F acting out.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3493 (Number of characters: 226):
The mindfulness processes described
below awaken the psyche’s innate capacity to respond with compassion to depression. Mindfulness also helps us metabolize the fear and shame that we were forced to feel about being
depressed.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3494 (Number of characters: 74):
Ending our reactivity to depression is typically a long difficult journey.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3495 (Number of characters: 339):
This is because our
culture routinely humiliates us for any expression of depression. It pathologizes us as if we are
violating our patriotic duty to be fully engaged in “the pursuit of happiness.”
Taboos about depression even emanate from the psychological establishment, where some
schools strip it of its status as a legitimate feeling.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3496 (Number of characters: 79):
For them, depression is nothing more than a
waste product of negative thinking.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3497 (Number of characters: 152):
Other schools reduce it simplistically to a dysfunctional state
that results from the repression of somewhat less taboo emotions like sadness and anger.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3498 (Number of characters: 62):
This is
not to say that these factors cannot cause depression.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3499 (Number of characters: 127):
It is to say that depression is a legitimate
feeling that often contains the helpful and important information described below.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3500 (Number of characters: 197):
Depressed Thinking Versus Feeling Depressed
Healing progresses when we learn to distinguish depressed thinking [which we need to
eliminate] from depressed feeling [which we sometimes need to feel].

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3501 (Number of characters: 118):
Occasional feelings of
listlessness and anhedonia [inability to enjoy our usual pleasures] are normal and existential.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3502 (Number of characters: 82):
A
modicum of ennui and dissatisfaction are part of the price of admission to life.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3503 (Number of characters: 420):
Moreover, depression is sometimes an invaluable herald of the need to slow down for rest
and restoration. When depression is most helpful, it gives us access to a unique spring of
intuition, such as that which informs us that a once valued job or relationship is no longer healthy
for us. In such instances we feel depressed because some irreparable change has rendered some
central thing in our lives detrimental to us.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3504 (Number of characters: 69):
This functional depression is signaling us to let it die
and move on.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3505 (Number of characters: 171):
Overreaction to depression essentially reinforces learned toxic shame. It reinforces the
person’s belief that he is unworthy, defective and unlovable when he is depressed.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3506 (Number of characters: 79):
Sadly this typically drives him deeper into abandonment-exacerbating isolation.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3507 (Number of characters: 176):
Mindfulness Metabolizes Depression

Deep level recovery from childhood trauma requires a normalization of depression, a
renunciation of the habit of reflexively reacting to it.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3508 (Number of characters: 72):
Central to this is the development of a self-
compassionate mindfulness.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3509 (Number of characters: 314):
Once again, mindfulness is the practice of staying in your body –
the practice of staying fully present to all of your internal experience. The practice of mindfulness cultivates our ability to stay acceptingly open to our emotional,
visceral and somatic experience without retreating into the cycle of reactivity.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3510 (Number of characters: 147):
Steven Levine’s
beautiful book, Who Dies, is an enlightening, jargon-less and easily accessible guide book on
learning the practice of mindfulness.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3511 (Number of characters: 19):
Somatic Mindfulness

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3512 (Number of characters: 164):
Because depression in Cptsd commonly morphs instantly into fear, early mindfulness work
involves staying present to the physical sensations of fearful hyperarousal.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3513 (Number of characters: 115):
This technique is also
known as sensate focusing and it is the practice of feeling we explored in the last chapter.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3514 (Number of characters: 336):
Mild
sensations of fear are muscular tightness or tension anywhere in the body, especially in the
alimentary canal. Tension in the jaw, throat, chest, diaphragm or belly also often correlates with
fear. More intense sensations of fear are nausea, jumpiness, feeling wired, shortness of breath,
hyperventilation, and alimentary distress.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3515 (Number of characters: 107):
Mindfulness also involves noticing the psyche’s penchant to dissociate from these
uncomfortable sensations.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3516 (Number of characters: 175):
Once again, dissociation can be the classical right-brain distraction of
spacing out into reverie or sleep, or the left-brain, cognitive distraction of worrying and
obsessing.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3517 (Number of characters: 157):
Over and over, the survivor will need to rescue himself from dissociation, and gently bring
his awareness back into fully feeling the sensations of his fear.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3518 (Number of characters: 197):
Although sensations of fear
sometimes feel unbearable at first, persistent focusing with non-reactive attention dissolves and
resolves them as if awareness itself is digesting and integrating them.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3519 (Number of characters: 208):
Somatic Awareness Can Therapeutically Trigger Painful Memories
Somatic awareness and sensate focusing sometimes opens up memories and unworked through
feelings of grief about your childhood abuse and neglect.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3520 (Number of characters: 108):
This phenomenon provides invaluable,
therapeutic opportunities to more fully grieve the losses of childhood.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3521 (Number of characters: 134):
If more pain comes up
then you can digest on your own, please consider getting someone more experienced to help you
with this process.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3522 (Number of characters: 123):
With considerable practice, you can begin to exhume from your fear an awareness of the
underlying sensations of depression.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3523 (Number of characters: 82):
These are hypo-aroused sensations that are subtle and
barely perceptible at first.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3524 (Number of characters: 103):
They may include heaviness, swollenness, exhaustion, emptiness,
hunger, longing, soreness, or deadness.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3525 (Number of characters: 80):
These sensations are initially as difficult to stay present to as those of fear.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3526 (Number of characters: 109):
With ongoing
practice however, focused attention also digests them as they are integrated into consciousness.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3527 (Number of characters: 98):
One of the biggest challenges of mindfully focusing on depression is to not dissociate into sleep.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3528 (Number of characters: 132):
Sitting up straight in a comfortable chair can help to keep you awake and focused on fully
feeling and metabolizing your depression.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3529 (Number of characters: 137):
As practice becomes more accomplished, these feelings and sensations of depression can

morph into a sense of peace, relaxation and ease.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3530 (Number of characters: 130):
On special occasions, they sometimes open to
an underlying, innate core emotional experience of clarity, well-being and belonging.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3531 (Number of characters: 26):
Introspective Somatic Work

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3532 (Number of characters: 110):
I began my journey of learning how to feel by devoting a half hour daily to feeling the sensations
of my fear.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3533 (Number of characters: 89):
This was extremely difficult because I had survived my childhood with ADHD-like
busyness.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3534 (Number of characters: 113):

--------------------------------------------------

--------------------------------------------------

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3538 (Number of characters: 142):

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3539 (Number of characters: 76):

catastrophizing thoughts and visualizations.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3540 (Number of characters: 96):

--------------------------------------------------

that I had strange tantalizing fantasies of cleaning my apartment, normally one of my least

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3544 (Number of characters: 96):
Otherwise, I would have been off
and running into self-medicating with excessive adrenalization.

chunk 3545 (Number of characters: 102):
Dissolving Depression By Fully Feeling It
Gradually, my focused awareness began to metabolize my fear.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3546 (Number of characters: 112):

typically able to relax the tense fearful sensations of a flashback within ten minutes.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3547 (Number of characters: 285):
As I became
more successful at digesting my fear, I experientially discovered the rock bottom underlying core
sensations of my abandonment depression itself. Mindful merging with the subtle emotions and sensations of depression is the finishing tool
of deconstructing self-abandonment.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3548 (Number of characters: 59):
Over and over I focused on the sensations of my
depression.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3549 (Number of characters: 81):
Occasionally these sensations were intense, but more often they were very subtle.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3550 (Number of characters: 85):
Often it was difficult to resist the urge to fall asleep, and many times I could not.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3551 (Number of characters: 159):
At other times, I noticed how instantly my depression scared me back into fear. Simultaneously the sensations of depression also triggered me into toxic shame.

--------------------------------------------------

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3553 (Number of characters: 46):

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3555 (Number of characters: 102):

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3556 (Number of characters: 154):

practiced and developed, is the ultimate thought-stopping technique.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3557 (Number of characters: 165):
When the critic is especially

loud and persistent, shifting your awareness from thinking to feeling your sensations is a potent
way of coming home to a safer place.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3558 (Number of characters: 81):
It is, as one survivor told me, “coming home to rest in the
mother of your body.”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3559 (Number of characters: 121):
In my current recovery work, I most commonly apply this mindfulness technique to

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3560 (Number of characters: 196):
For me this low grade manifestation of the

decreases my energy level and dulls my ability to concentrate.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3561 (Number of characters: 76):
Most of my life, this feeling

--------------------------------------------------

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3564 (Number of characters: 243):
I have learned how to
resist my critic’s all-or-none productivity program, and be satisfied with days of getting less
done.
Hunger As Camouflaged Depression
Feelings of abandonment commonly masquerade as the physiological sensations of hunger.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3565 (Number of characters: 61):
Hunger pain soon after a big meal is rarely truly about food.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3566 (Number of characters: 92):
Typically it is camouflaged
emotional hunger and the longing for safe, nurturing connection.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3567 (Number of characters: 51):
Food cannot satiate the hunger
pain of abandonment.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3568 (Number of characters: 24):
Only loving support can.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3569 (Number of characters: 66):
Geneen Roth’s book offers powerful self-help
book on this subject.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3570 (Number of characters: 320):
Even after a decade of practice, I still find it difficult to differentiate this type of attachment
hunger from physical hunger. One often reliable clue is that the sensation of longing for the
nourishment of attachment is usually in my small intestine, while physical hunger’s locus is a
little higher up in my stomach.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3571 (Number of characters: 230):
When I can take the time to meditate on this lower abdomen
sensation, I often become aware that I am in a low level flashback. Feeling through this
uncomfortable sensation typically resolves both the flashback and my false hunger.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3572 (Number of characters: 221):
I believe this type of emotional hunger is at the core of most food addictions. One of the
reasons food addictions are so difficult to manage is that food was the first source of self-
comforting that was available to us.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3573 (Number of characters: 112):
With the dearth of any other comfort, there is little wonder
that we came to over-rely on eating for nurturance.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3574 (Number of characters: 126):
In fact, like Gabor Mate, I believe that attachment hunger is at the core of most addictive
behavior, even process addictions.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3575 (Number of characters: 99):
An example of the latter is the sex and love addict’s desperate
pursuit of high intensity relating.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3576 (Number of characters: 125):
Perhaps all addictive behavior is our misguided attempts to
self-medicate deeper abandonment pain and unmet attachment needs.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3577 (Number of characters: 18):
Pseudo-Cyclothymia

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3578 (Number of characters: 91):
On a parallel with false hunger, feeling tired is sometimes unrelated to sleep deprivation.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3579 (Number of characters: 68):
It is
instead an emotional experience of the abandonment depression.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3580 (Number of characters: 190):
I believe that emotional
tiredness comes from not resting enough in a safe relationship with yourself or with another. This
emotional exhaustion often masquerades as physiological tiredness.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3581 (Number of characters: 69):
Unfortunately, over time, the
two can become confusingly intertwined.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3582 (Number of characters: 100):
When our abandonment depression is unremediated, either kind of tiredness can trigger us

into fear.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3583 (Number of characters: 145):
This then activates the inner critic which then translates “tired” into “endangering
imperfection,” which in turn triggers us into a 4F response.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3584 (Number of characters: 288):
Ironically, over-reacting to emotional tiredness eventually creates real physical exhaustion
via a process I call The Cyclothymic Two-Step. The cyclothymic two-step is the dance of flight
types or subtypes who habitually overreact to their tiredness with workaholic or busyholic
activity.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3585 (Number of characters: 133):
Self-medicating with their own adrenalin, they “run” to counteract the emotional
tiredness of the unprocessed abandonment depression.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3586 (Number of characters: 120):
Eventually however, many exhaust themselves physically, and become temporarily too
depleted or sick to continue running.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3587 (Number of characters: 167):
At such times, they collapse into an accumulated depression
so painful, that they re-launch desperately into “flight-speed” at the first sign of replenished
adrenalin.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3588 (Number of characters: 166):
Survivors with this pattern sometimes misdiagnose themselves as bipolar because of
their abrupt vacillations between adrenalin highs and abandonment-exacerbated lows.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3589 (Number of characters: 135):
Also noteworthy here is the futile journey that many survivors undergo treating emotional
tiredness with physiologically-based methods.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3590 (Number of characters: 103):
The limited efficacy of such an approach however
typically augments their shame: “What’s wrong with me.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3591 (Number of characters: 72):
I’ve changed everything in my diet and
in my sleep and exercise regimen.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3592 (Number of characters: 126):
I’ve taken every available supplement and seen every type of
practitioner imaginable, and I still wake up feeling dead tired.”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3593 (Number of characters: 70):
There is a healthy way out of this cul-de-sac of misdirected striving.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3594 (Number of characters: 114):
It lies in cultivating
self-kindness during those inevitable times when you feel tired, bad, lonely, or depressed.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3595 (Number of characters: 101):
In this
regard, the notable AA 12 Step acronym, HALT - Hungry, Angry, Lonely, Tired – can be
helpful.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3596 (Number of characters: 150):
Accordingly, I recommend that you focus inside to see if you have flashed back into the
abandonment depression whenever you experience a HALT feeling.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3597 (Number of characters: 102):
If you have, you can then
work to generate the internal, self-compassionate attention described above.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3598 (Number of characters: 227):
Separating Necessary Suffering From Unnecessary Suffering
You can sometimes gain motivation for this difficult work by seeing your depressed feelings as
messages from your inner child who is feeling as abandoned as he ever was.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3599 (Number of characters: 105):
Perhaps this time
you can come to him with your remothering-self in a more comforting and protective way.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3600 (Number of characters: 299):
Through such practice, you can gradually achieve the healing that Buddhists call separating
necessary suffering [normal fear and depression] from unnecessary suffering [unconscious self-
abandonment to helplessness, toxic shame, Cptsd fear, retraumatizing inner critic acting in, and
4F acting out].

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3601 (Number of characters: 156):
Recovery Is Progressive
I begin this section by repeating the chapter 4 section: “Stages of Recovery.” This section
outlines the overall course of recovery.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3602 (Number of characters: 127):
Hopefully you will notice, since you began reading this
book, that your understanding of Cptsd has been significantly enhanced.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3603 (Number of characters: 101):
I also hope that you
sense a greater sense of self-compassion and direction in your recovery process.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3604 (Number of characters: 113):
“Although we often work on many levels of recovering at the same time, recovering is to some

degree progressive.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3605 (Number of characters: 109):
It begins on the cognitive level when psychoeducation and mindfulness helps
us understand that we have Cptsd.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3606 (Number of characters: 129):
This awakening then allows us to learn how to approach the
journey of deconstructing the various life-spoiling dynamics of Cptsd.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3607 (Number of characters: 96):
Still on the cognitive level, we take our next steps into the long work of shrinking the critic.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3608 (Number of characters: 168):
Some survivors will need to do a great deal of work on this level before they can move down to
the emotional layer of work, which is learning how to grieve effectively.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3609 (Number of characters: 202):
The phase of intensely grieving our childhood losses can last for a couple of years. When
sufficient progress is made in grieving, the survivor naturally drops down into the next level of
recovery work.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3610 (Number of characters: 79):
This involves working through fear by grieving our loss of safety in the world.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3611 (Number of characters: 101):
At
this level, we also learn to work through our toxic shame by grieving the loss of our self-esteem.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3612 (Number of characters: 154):
As we become more adept in this type of deep level grieving, we are then ready to address
the core issue of our Cptsd – the abandonment depression itself.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3613 (Number of characters: 170):
The final task here involves
releasing the armoring and physiological reactivity in our body to the abandonment depression
via the somatic work discussed in this chapter.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3614 (Number of characters: 110):
This work culminates with learning to
compassionately support ourselves through our experiences of depression.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3615 (Number of characters: 194):
Finally, as we will see in the next chapter, many survivors need some relational help in
achieving the complex tasks involved in deconstructing each layer of our old pain-exacerbating
defenses.”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3616 (Number of characters: 135):
A Swiss Army Knife Approach To A Flashback
This is a final example of addressing all the layers of reactivity in resolving a flashback.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3617 (Number of characters: 275):
When I started this work over three decades ago, I ate very poorly and did not know how to
soothe and nurture myself with healthy food. After some years of recovery work however, I deeply intuited that I needed to cook more in
order to take self-nurturance to the next level.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3618 (Number of characters: 54):
I had not cooked more however because I hated to
cook.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3619 (Number of characters: 143):
Luckily I embraced cooking anyway as a labor of love. Nonetheless cooking was, for a
long time, a very trying and unpleasant experience for me.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3620 (Number of characters: 95):
[This was, in fact, much like my
many years of struggling to get the upper hand on the critic].

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3621 (Number of characters: 77):
But back to cooking which became a central piece of my self-remothering work.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3622 (Number of characters: 136):
It was not
until I understood more about the dynamics of my flight response that I realized how much I
rushed around when I was cooking.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3623 (Number of characters: 142):
Via increased mindfulness, I discovered that the smallest
unforeseen obstacle could set off what felt like a small electric shock in my chest.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3624 (Number of characters: 160):
Examples of
these obstacles include something spilled, a lid too tight to open, an extra unanticipated task, or
the clock showing me that I was behind schedule.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3625 (Number of characters: 111):
In an instant, anyone of these normal
hindrances could send me rushing around the kitchen in a low grade panic.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3626 (Number of characters: 109):
Whenever this
occurred, I would then inhale my meal as soon as it was ready just to get the ordeal over with.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3627 (Number of characters: 236):
This is an example of what a common, everyday flashback looks like in adulthood. It is so
often minor daily frustrations that trigger us into flashbacks, rather than incidents that repeat the
gross insults and ordeals of our childhoods.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3628 (Number of characters: 168):
With ongoing recovery work, I realized that anything to do with food could easily flash me
back to the family dinner table - the battlefield of my dysfunctional family.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3629 (Number of characters: 51):
Many productive grieving sessions came out of this.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3630 (Number of characters: 142):
They lead me to an epiphany that

doing anything intricate with lot of steps to it also flashed me back to feeling picked apart by my
parents.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3631 (Number of characters: 142):
I subsequently discovered that angering at my parents, whenever I was triggered by
doing something complicated, significantly reduced my fear.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3632 (Number of characters: 133):
There was, however, a huge part of my food trauma that I could not shrink until I started the
somatic work described in this chapter.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3633 (Number of characters: 115):
Since then, years of practicing somatic mindfulness has greatly reduced my hair-triggerable
anxiety around cooking.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3634 (Number of characters: 41):
This is however still a work in progress.

--------------------------------------------------

reactivity, my occasional flashbacks around food and food preparation can look like this.

chair.

chunk 3683 (Number of characters: 146):
A RELATIONAL APPOACH TO HEALING ABANDONMENT
This chapter is a reworking of a professional article I wrote to guide therapists in their Cptsd
work.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3684 (Number of characters: 352):
I have kept that point of reference in many sections of this chapter in the hope that it will
help you know what it is reasonable to expect from a therapist. Further on in this chapter, there is a section on how to find a therapist. I hope this
information will help you know what to look for, and ask for, when and if you are shopping for a
therapist.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3685 (Number of characters: 140):
Finally, if therapy is not an option for you, I end the chapter with guidelines on how to
create a co-counseling relationship with a friend.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3686 (Number of characters: 152):
If that also is not possible, I list
recommendations for online forums where you can interact with others who are sharing about
their recovery journeys.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3687 (Number of characters: 107):
The Relational Dimension Of Psychotherapy
Many Cptsd survivors have never had a “safe enough” relationship.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3688 (Number of characters: 217):
Healing our attachment
disorders usually requires a reparative relational experience with a therapist, partner or trusted
friend who is able to stay compassionately present to their own painful and dysphoric feelings.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3689 (Number of characters: 123):
It
is essential that they are comfortable with feeling and expressing their own sadness, anger, fear,
shame and depression.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3690 (Number of characters: 165):
When a therapist has this level of emotional intelligence, she can guide the client to
gradually let go of the learned habit of automatically rejecting his feelings.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3691 (Number of characters: 110):
This in turn also helps
him to avoid getting lost in the cycles of reactivity we explored in the last chapter.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3692 (Number of characters: 181):
Safe and empathic eye and voice connection with a therapist who has “good enough”
emotional intelligence models to the client how to stay acceptingly present to all her own affects.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3693 (Number of characters: 54):
Daniel Siegel calls this the “coregulation of affect.”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3694 (Number of characters: 189):
Moreover, Susan Vaughan’s work
demonstrates that this coregulation of affect promotes the development of the inner neural

circuitry necessary to metabolize overwhelmingly painful feelings.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3695 (Number of characters: 180):
Furthermore, there is increasing neuroscientific evidence suggesting that this process is
physiologically accomplished through the agency of a set of neurons called mirror neurons.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3696 (Number of characters: 111):
In
one experiment measuring neural activity in two monkeys, one monkey watched as the other
cracked open a nut.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3697 (Number of characters: 63):
The observer’s neural activity was identical to the performers.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3698 (Number of characters: 122):
Perhaps
mirror neurons are involved when the client learns to be as non-reactive to his painful feelings as
the therapist.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3699 (Number of characters: 432):
RELATIONAL HEALING IN COMPLEX PTSD
[Versions of the following article were first published in The Therapist and The East Bay
Therapist.] Many traumatologists see attachment disorder as one of the key symptoms of Complex PTSD. In
the psychoeducational phases of working with traumatized clients, I typically describe
attachment disorder as the result of growing up with primary caretakers who were regularly
experienced as dangerous.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3700 (Number of characters: 119):
They were dangerous by contemptuous voice or heavy hand, or more
insidiously, dangerous by remoteness and indifference.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3701 (Number of characters: 105):
Recurring abuse and neglect habituates children to living in fear and sympathetic nervous
system arousal.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3702 (Number of characters: 156):
It makes them easily triggerable into the abandonment mélange of overwhelming
fear and shame that tangles up with the depressed feelings of being abandoned.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3703 (Number of characters: 144):
A child, with parents who are unable or unwilling to provide safe enough attachment, has
no one to whom she can bring her whole developing self.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3704 (Number of characters: 56):
No one is there for reflection, validation and guidance.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3705 (Number of characters: 71):
No one is safe enough to go to for
comfort or help in times of trouble.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3706 (Number of characters: 127):
There is no one to cry to, to protest unfairness to, and to seek
compassion from for hurts, mistakes, accidents, and betrayals.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3707 (Number of characters: 107):
No one is safe enough to shine with, to do “show and tell” with, and to be reflected as a
subject of pride.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3708 (Number of characters: 92):
There is no one to even practice the all-important intimacy-building skills of
conversation.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3709 (Number of characters: 113):
In the paraphrased words of more than one of my clients: “Talking to Mom was like giving
ammunition to the enemy.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3710 (Number of characters: 51):
Anything I said could and would be used against me.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3711 (Number of characters: 83):
No wonder,
people always tell me that I don’t seem to have much to say for myself.”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3712 (Number of characters: 132):
Those with Cptsd-spawned attachment disorders never learn the communication skills that
engender closeness and a sense of belonging.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3713 (Number of characters: 159):
When it comes to relating, they are often plagued
by debilitating social anxiety - and social phobia when they are at the severe end of the
continuum of Cptsd.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3714 (Number of characters: 87):
Many of the clients who come through my door have never had a safe enough relationship.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3715 (Number of characters: 183):
Repetition compulsion drives them to unconsciously seek out relationships in adulthood that
traumatically reenact the abusive and/or abandoning dynamics of their childhood caretakers.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3716 (Number of characters: 95):
For many such clients, we are their first legitimate shot at a safe and nurturing relationship.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3717 (Number of characters: 155):
If we are not skilled enough to create the degree of safety they need to begin the long journey
towards developing good enough trust, we may be their last.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3718 (Number of characters: 112):
Emotional flashback management, therefore, is empowered when it is taught in the context
of a safe relationship.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3719 (Number of characters: 251):
Clients need to feel safe enough with their therapist to describe their

humiliation and overwhelm. At the same time, the therapist needs to be nurturing enough to
provide the empathy and calm support that was missing in the client’s early experience.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3720 (Number of characters: 161):
Just as importantly, the therapist needs to be able to tolerate and work therapeutically with
the sudden evaporation of trust that is so characteristic of Cptsd.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3721 (Number of characters: 152):
Trauma survivors do not have a
volitional “on” switch for trust, even though their “off” switch is frequently automatically
triggered during flashbacks.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3722 (Number of characters: 248):
In therapy, the therapist must be able to work on reassurance and
trust restoral over and over again. I have heard too many disappointing client stories about past
therapists who got angry at them because they would not simply choose to trust them.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3723 (Number of characters: 119):
As the importance of this understanding ripens in me, I increasingly embrace an Intersubjective
or Relational approach.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3724 (Number of characters: 243):
That means that I believe that the quality of the clients’ relationship with
me can provide a corrective emotional experience that saves them from being doomed to a
lifetime of superficial connection, or worse, social isolation and alienation.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3725 (Number of characters: 210):
Moreover, I notice that without the development of a modicum of trust with me, my Cptsd
clients are seriously delimited in their receptivity to my guidance, as well as to the ameliorative
effects of my empathy.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3726 (Number of characters: 188):
In this regard then, I will describe four key qualities of relating that I believe are essential to
the development of trust, and the subsequent relational healing that can come out of it.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3727 (Number of characters: 96):
These are
Empathy, Authentic Vulnerability, Dialogicality and Collaborative Relationship Repair.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3728 (Number of characters: 10):
1. EMPATHY

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3729 (Number of characters: 130):
I used to assume that the merits of empathy were a given, but I have sadly heard too many stories
of empathy-impoverished therapy.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3730 (Number of characters: 196):
In this regard, I will simply say here that if we are hard and
unsympathetic with our clients, we trigger the same sense of danger and abandonment in them
that they experienced with their parents.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3731 (Number of characters: 187):
In terms of a definition, I especially like Kohut’s statement that: “Empathy involves
immersing yourself in another’s psychological state by feeling yourself into the other’s
experience.”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3732 (Number of characters: 225):
When I delve deeply enough into a client’s experience, no matter how initially perplexing
or intemperate it may at first seem, I inevitably find psychological sense in it, especially when I
recognize its flashback components.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3733 (Number of characters: 160):
In fact, I can honestly say that I have never met a feeling or
behavior that did not make sense when viewed through the lenses of transference and
traumatology.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3734 (Number of characters: 516):
Empathy, of course, deepens via careful listening and full elicitation of the client’s
experience, along with the time-honored techniques of mirroring and paraphrasing which show
the client the degree to which we get him. Noticing my subjective free associations often enhances my empathic attunement and
ability to reflect back to the client in an emotionally accurate and validating way. When
appropriate, I sometimes share my autobiographical free associations with the client when they
are emotionally analogous.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3735 (Number of characters: 75):
I do this to let her know that I really empathize with what she is
sharing.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3736 (Number of characters: 27):
This is an example of this.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3737 (Number of characters: 114):
My client tells me with great embarrassment that she stayed
home all weekend because she had a pimple on her nose.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3738 (Number of characters: 59):
She is ashamed of the pimple and of
her “vanity’’ about it.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3739 (Number of characters: 75):
She moans: “How could I be so stupid to let such a little thing bother me?”

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3740 (Number of characters: 66):
I suddenly remember cancelling a date once when I had a cold sore.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3741 (Number of characters: 53):
At the time, I also got lost in
a toxic shame attack.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3742 (Number of characters: 56):
I share this with her, minus present day shame about it.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3743 (Number of characters: 63):
She tears up and
then laughs, relieved as her shame melts away.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3744 (Number of characters: 74):
Months later, she tells me that her trust in me
mushroomed at that moment.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3745 (Number of characters: 90):
Guidelines for being judicious about this kind of self-disclosure
will be discussed below.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3746 (Number of characters: 131):
Of the many benefits of empathy, the greatest is perhaps that it models and teaches self-
empathy, better known as self-acceptance.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3747 (Number of characters: 150):
To the degree that we attune to and welcome all of the
client’s experience, to that same degree can the client learn to welcome it in her- or himself.

--------------------------------------------------

chunk 3748 (Number of characters: 2):
2.
